For this very reason you get to see a lot more of the characters that narrate the story, understand their feelings, and see how they go about everyday life. While I enjoyed the majority of these chapters, I will say that a few could have been left out. I found myself forcing my way through these select chapters in a way that I never did before. For this reason I give this book a 4 instead of a 5.<br /><br />Overall, this is still an excellent book in the series. It isn't as action packed as book 3, but the slower pace and fewer POV characters will give you a better grip on the world that Martin created. The Ironborn and Dornish chapters are(for the most part) very interesting and a change of pace from the main story. Some parts of the book slow down but it certainly ends with a bang that readers will not want to miss. My advice is don't listen to the negative reviews and read this one for yourself. I was also skeptical, but my love for the series pushed me to read it and i'm glad I did.

B003XF1XOQ|2.0|My 2 star review is as much for the whole series as this final book.  The Hunger Games started out well enough, but Catching Fire was nothing more than a bridge novel and by the third book Katpiss has devolved into an unlikeable wench.  Given that The Hunger Games movie was so successful, I have no idea how they can follow it up with anything - unless the plot and characters are revised, the next films won't live up to the hype.  Well... I suppose that the subjective teen girl audience will no doubt applaud one way or another.<br /><br />To start off, the leaders of District 13 want to make Katniss into a symbol of the revolution - a propaganda tool.  In order to get the message out, they dress her up, put her on stage, and attempt to shoot some footage of her saying some lines to inspire the troops.  She basically lays an egg and they call off the production.<br /><br />Ok... so they are only doing one take?  They aren't going to give it another try?<br /><br />So what do they decide to do after ONE try at having her say some lines on camera?  The leaders of District 13 and Haymitch decide that they would get better footage if they put her in combat situations.  Uhhhhhhhh....ok.<br /><br />Meanwhile, President Snow has nothing better to do with his time than concern himself with a reality show contestant.  Katniss is basically just like a winner of Survivor, yet the President makes it a top priority to make her life miserable - torturing Peeta and such.  Really?  There is a war going on and Snow has nothing better to so than to focus his efforts on a propaganda tool for the resistance?  Snow bombs District 13 and goes out of his way to leave flowers as a message for Katniss?  Give me a break.<br /><br />Frankly, I found Katniss to be absolutely self-centered and annoying.  Everything is about her.  Her clothes... her hair... her potential boyfriends... her family... Heck, even her desire to rescue Peeta is really about her.  Is Katniss happy when Fennick is reunited with his girl?  No... she is jealous.<br /><br />Seriously now... think about this... does Katniss really care about the resistance and the war?  OR, does she really just care about herself?  What is her motivation?  Is her motivation to free the districts from tyranny, or is it to save herself and her friends?  Is she a selfless hero willing to sacrifice herself for the cause, or is she merely doing what she has to until she comes up with more options?  Is she leading the charge, or is she just being swept along by the tide?  Is she interested in the welfare of the people, or just her obsession with killing Snow herself?<br /><br />For example, think about her reaction to Peeta's torture at the hands of Snow.  On page 179, Katniss thinks, \\"I cover my face with my arms because this isn't happening.  It isn't possible.  For someone to make Peeta forget that he loves me... no one could do that.\\"<br /><br />Dude!  Are you kidding me?  The guy just went through weeks or torture and your biggest concern is that he might not love you anymore?  It is Prim, not Katniss, that expresses some concern over Peeta and his state.  Katniss, like usual, just focuses on her own feelings.  How could Peeta stop loving me?  I'm sooooo special.<br /><br />Well - apparently Peeta isn't that big of a deal, because within a few days Katniss is making out with Gale again.  On page 196, Katniss kisses Gale and thinks, \\"despite what I feel for Peeta, this is when I accept deep down that he'll never come back to me.  Or I'll never go back to him.\\"<br /><br />Wow.  I guess that's all it takes huh?  Gale sticks his tongue down her throat and Peeta is washed up.  But that's Katniss for you - Peeta isn't interested anymore, so too bad for him.  After all, it is all about who loves Katniss... this rebellion stuff just gets in the way.<br /><br />Perhaps you think I'm just being negative and unrealistic.  Not so - other characters in the book point out what a train wreck Katniss is.  Johanna tellsKatniss she is \\"hard to swallow\\" with all of the \\"tacky romantic drama.\\"<br /><br />Katniss even refers to herself as violent, distrustful, and manipulative.<br /><br />Another example on page 266.  Haymitch calls Katniss out for how poorly she is treating Peeta, \\"If you'd been taken by the Capitol, and hijacked, and then tried to kill Peeta, is this the way he would be treating you?\\"  Katniss has to admit to herself that it is not.  \\"He would be trying to get me back at any cost.  Not shutting me out, abandoning me, greeting me with hostility at every turn.\\"<br /><br />And why does Katpiss act like this?  Because she is a self-centered B that's why.  An entirely unlikeable character.<br /><br />More?  Page 327 - Peeta and Gale are discussing who Katniss will choose and Gale says, \\"Katniss will pick whoever she thinks she can't survive without.\\"  This statement disturbs Katniss, who thinks \\"am I really that cold and calculating?\\"  She gets upset because Gale's comment implies no passion or feeling - rather that Katniss is simply conducting an unfeeling assessment.<br /><br />Katniss aside, the \\"end game\\" of the book gets really stupid when Katniss takes command of a group of soldiers and decides to invade the Capitol herself in order to assassinate Snow.  Wow... I mean heck, why wouldn't you invade the Capitol by yourself?  Gee - if it was that easy why didn't anyone do it before?  Oh wait... sorry... I'm not supposed to be thinking about that...<br /><br />Anyway, as Katniss and her dwindling team progress into the Capitol, so do the rest of the rebel forces.  Where did they come from?  Who knows.  Was there any plan to invade the Capitol at this time?  Who knows.  How is the Capitol defended?  Who knows.  Why did the Capitol basically plant \\"pod\\" bombs all over its own streets?  Who knows.  Where did they get squads of mutants to track people down?  Who knows.  Does the reader really have any idea what is going on other than what is in the head of Katniss?  No.  Like I said in the beginning, this book reads like a personal journal of her emotional problems.<br /><br />Sometimes when reading a book, you can sense when the wheels fall off the cart and the author is looking for a way to just get it over.  That's how I felt reading the last few chapters of Mockingjay.  A senseless progression of absurdity leading up to the conclusion.
1455869880|5.0|This is a library audio CD version of the book. I think had I not listened to the book, narrated by Kate Rudd, I would have given up early. The characters are pretentious, unreal, and unlikable. But I loved Kate's voice and her ability to bring life into the story. It is amazing how soon I was pulled in, in spite of myself, and cared deeply for the characters.<br /><br />As has been mentioned in previous reviews, the book description, and my tags, this is a book about teens who have cancer. As if being a teen isn't difficult enough to go through, add to it, dying. So, yes, this is a bring your Kleenex book. But it is bittersweet and you may find yourself laughing at the antics of the characters and their lighthearted take on the serious situations they find themselves in.<br /><br />Would all teens with cancer like this book? Would all young adults? I don't know. I am 62 and I loved it! Not the subject matter. I lost my mother to cancer. It isn't pretty. But we all deal with life and what it hands us differently. But for Hazel, Augustus and Isaac, this is their lives and how they choose to live and die. Philosophies of life and love are examined and either embraced or rejected and sometimes both.<br /><br />I may eventually buy the Audible version and the Kindle version as I feel there is a lot to learn about writing within these pages. I would like to see how I'll imagine the voices/personalities without Kate Rudd's voice.<br /><br />What is special about this version is the interview with John Green at the end of the book. He talks about the writing process attached to this book. He, also, said that he made an audio version with his own voice. That would be fun to see how he would interpret his own words.<br /><br />I think it is a great read, but I wouldn't go so far as to make it a mandatory book for high-schoolers. In fact, I would caution the reader to choose a time in their lives when they feel stable to then savor this book.

B001KVZ6HK|5.0|Review from MetalArcade.net<br /><br />\\"The best Hulk movie yet.\\" That's what a lot of people are saying about The Avengers, and while that is absolutely true, it's much more than that. It's an incredibly fun film, overflowing with action and yet still making time for real character development, and even some truly great comedy. I tip my metaphorical hat to Joss Whedon, who has pulled off one of the most exciting and engaging superhero movies I've ever seen.<br /><br />Whedon is a certifiable geek genius, and his passion shines through in every frame of this film. Having helmed Buffy and Firefly, he's become very adept at bringing a team together and giving them some heart. There's no better example than in the fact that The Hulk was one of my favorite characters in this movie, when I've previously only seen him as boring and one-dimensional. I have to give credit to Mark Ruffalo as well, for making this character very watchable rather than groan-inducing.<br /><br />If you've seen any Marvel film in the past few years, you know full well that Sam Jackson's Nick Fury has been trying to wrangle together a super group of superheroes together for his Avengers Initiative. The film has a very basic premise- Thor's Loki (Tom Hiddleston) has attacked the Earth with an alien army, and the only way to stop him is to get the band onstage - that would be The Hulk (Mark Ruffalo), Thor (Chris Hemsworth), Black Widow (Scarlett Johansson), Captain America (Chris Evans), Iron Man (Robert Downey, Jr.), and Hawkeye (Jeremy Renner).<br /><br />That's a lot of characters to balance, and I went into The Avengers fully expecting the film to be cluttered, confusing and disjointed. It's a small miracle that the film is not only easy to follow, but incredibly fun to watch. The dialogue between the team (especially when they first meet) is excellent and often hilarious, and never feels forced. Nobody feels shoehorned in, and there are so many epic action sequences that I almost felt worn out by the end of the film's runtime. The theater I saw it in was packed to the brim (much like the film itself), and although I had to crane my neck the entire time, I couldn't take my eyes off the screen. It almost feels like a lucid dream, speaking as a comic fan, because even though the film has been hyped for years, it's difficult to comprehend the awesomeness of seeing all these great characters (and actors) on the same screen. It's a bit cliche, but it is the closest thing I've yet seen to a comic book come to life, and it's every bit as awesome as I hoped. It's just an exceedingly fun movie, which is very rare these days.<br /><br />Every character is at their best in this film. Captain America is as patriotic and no-nonsense as ever, and Iron Man's vicious wit is always hilarious and never overbearing. Ruffalo manages to imbue Bruce Banner with a few new layers of heart, and lends him a distinct personality that was sorely lacking from Hulk's previous film entries. We also learn what makes Black Widow tick, and she is genuinely interesting this time around, rather than just a ridiculously hot female in tight leather. Which I still would have been ok with, but at least women won't have any reason to complain about her- and she kicks a lot of ass in this movie.<br /><br />It's not spoiling anything (it's in every commercial) to tell you that there are a few battles among the heroes in this movie. I won't give away who fights who, but rest assured that these battles are even cooler than they were in the comics, and executed very well. They're also not just done for the sake of awesome fight scenes, as it realistically shows off the wildly differing personalities of the team, which definitely don't quite gel together at first.<br /><br />It's not necessary to watch all of the heroes' previous Marvel films to understand The Avengers, but it definitely gives the characters and their interactions more depth. I would say that Thor is the only must-see film before TheAvengers, as the films' villains are the same person, and his motivations, and the brother's personalities  become much clearer upon viewing that film. The members of the S.H.I.E.L.D. team, notably Maria Hill (Cobie Smulders), Nick Fury (Sam Jackson), and Agent Coulson (Clarke Gregg) all get much less screen time than the main cast, which is to be expected, but they all are handled well, with Coulson and Fury feeling much more fleshed out after viewing this film.<br /><br />Overall, The Avengers can be seen as nothing short of a huge accomplishment. Everyone in the cast gives their best performances thus far, and bounce off of each other perfectly. Joss Whedon infuses every scene with heart and excitement, and you'll be on the edge of your seat for the entire film. I can only hope that The Dark Knight Rises and The Amazing Spider-Man match my expectations as well as The Avengers has.Avengers, as the films' villains are the same person, and his motivations, and the brother's personalities  become much clearer upon viewing that film. B004A8ZWVK|5.0|The first thing that came to my mind the moment I began reading The Help by Kathryn Stockett was her vernacular. Kathryn Stockett is what you will term 'white', for lack of a better word. But it's amazing how she manages to fit into the day-to-day vernacular of a 'colored' woman. Her language is free-flowing, easy and natural, and no where in the book does it feel like she is making an effort or any of it is made up. Full points to her for that.<br /><br /> The story is told from the point of view of three main female characters - Aibileen, the motherly, loving, intelligent colored maid, who works as a nanny and maid; Minnie, who is a sassy, sharp-tongued maid, but clearly one of the best cooks the white ladies have ever had; Miss Skeeter, a young white female who has recently returned from college, only to find that her beloved Constantine, the colored maid who raised her and took on the figure of a real mother for her, has suddenly disappeared, and who now has the passion to share her thoughts on what she feels is needed today to make this world a better place, and who clearly does not believe in racial discrimination and wants to do something to bring an end to it.<br /><br /> The Help is set in the 70s, in Jackson, Mississippi, a time when apartheid was rampant, especially in the areas mentioned in the book, and the discrimination between colored and white people was openly practised.<br /><br />The story:<br /> Aibileen is a mother-figure, a colored woman who has spent her life, from the time she was a pre-teen, in the house of 'white' people raising their babies - 17 to be precise. And for each of the babies she has raised, she has a special place in her heart. Some have turned out to be just the way she hoped, the babies she once held in her arms have turned out to be amazing as human beings, while a few have learnt the ways of their parents and grown up to discriminate the very race that brought them up with so much love and care. Aibileen has recently losta grown-up son, and she confesses that something inside her has now changed. Her son, educated and intelligent, was writing a book on being a black in America in the 70s, but being colored meant he could not get a decent job, and he was forced to work as a cargo man, hauling heavy stuff. It was on one such night that he got crushed and his white employer put him in a truck and once outside the colored hospital, rolled off his body and drove off. Aibileen's son died, taking away something from his mamma's life forever. As Aibileen begins her new job looking after Mae Mobley, the white baby of Miss Elizabeth Leefolt, we are taken into the home of the whites, to see the discrimination that is seeped in society and its culture.<br /><br /> The same maid who is deemed fit to raise a white infant and take care of her all needs is not seen fit to use a bathroom that white guests may use. A colored maid cannot sit at the same table to eat, she is not allowed to speak to a white person unless a white person talks to her, and even while handing out a coffee cup, a colored maid is expected to first place the cup on the table, so that the white person will not come into contact with the colored skin, even by accident.<br /><br /> Minnie is Aibileen's best friend, despite their age gap. A tough, colored woman, raising her brood of kids, living with a husband who is drunk and beats her up regularly, the pressures of providing for her children falls mainly on her. This is not easy, especially for Minnie, as she is notorious for having a sharp-tongue and a sassy attitude, one that lands her in trouble often. She does not hide her contempt for the way the whites treat the coloreds, and she is not afraid to speak her mind, many times at the cost of her job even.<br /><br /> Skeeter is a white who is now feeling alien in her own white world. She has recently returned to her family's plantation and mansion after completing college. While her friends busy themselves with clothes, shoes,bridge parties and gossip, all the while mistreating or ignoring the colored people around them, Skeeter finds it increasingly difficult to abide by these so-called 'white' rules of etiquette. She is appalled at the way the colored are being humiliated, and she finds many loopholes in a society that otherwise calls itself 'perfect' and 'classy.' Determined to become a journalist, Skeeter lands herself a job as a 'housekeeping advice columnist', and while she has no idea of anything related to housework, she resolves to find a house help who can help her with her own column. It is in this capacity that she gets to chat with Aibileen, her best friend Elizabeth's colored help.<br /><br /> As the story unfolds,<br />a strange friendship begins to form between Skeeter and Aibileen. Not a friendship in the beginning, it is more of a relationship that sees both women trying to understand the world of the other. While Skeeter wants to overcome the racial boundaries and tries to be friends with Aibileen and try to understand what she must be going through with so much discrimination and ill-treatment each day, Aibileen wants to figure out how a white woman suddenly is trying to reach out to her, trying to be friends, asking after her life and her feelings!<br /><br /> As Skeeter begins to see more of Aibileen, she enters a circle that was till now invisible to her. While interacting with her best friends Elizabeth and Hilly, she begins noticing their attitude towards their maids, and the way they treat them. She also remembers her own upbringing at the hands of her colored maid, Constantine, and realises that Constantine must have faced many such humiliations too, even though she never complained. When Skeeter sends a job request to a publishing house editor, she is asked to write something fruitful, something that is different, something that catches the pulse of the society, something that talks about a 'change.' Interacting with Aibileen and seeing the attitudes of her friends, Skeeter realises soon what she is going to talk about.<br /><br /> What begins as a relationship between Skeeter and Aibileen as the former starts to write about her life and experiences, soon grows into a bond that both begin to cherish, and very soon, Minnie also joins the two, sharing her own experiences, and the trio form an unlikely bond, an unlikely friendship.<br /><br /> With racial discrimination growing and colored facing inhuman treatment at the hands of the whites, discontent is everywhere, and very soon, many colored women join Skeeter in the secret mission of writing about their lives and experiences.<br /><br /> When the book comes out, the entire white society is shaken up, and its effects are felt everywhere. At the time when Martin Luther King and apartheid were a burning truth in society, the topics that Skeeter, Aibileen and Minnie deal in are sure to come with their own burning results - and they do.<br /><br /> Characters:<br /> What I loved about the novel is Stockett's portrayal of the characters. While Aibileen, Minnie and Skeeter are clearly the protagonists, the antagonist in the story, Hilly Holbrook, is a character who is drawn to the last bit of detail. She is clearly meant to be hated, and Stockett manages to get just that out of her readers. By the end of the novel, I was cheering for Hilly's problems and humiliations - clearly doing exactly what the author intended me to do. The other character who is extremely adorable even in her quirky ways, is Celia Foote, the 'yellow-haired' bimbo, who is termed as 'white trash' by the society ladies and is made into a social outcast, even despite her many attempts to gel into society and be a part of the Jackson elites. While others derogate Celia for her 'vulgar' ways, we get to see her attitude towards her 'help', whom not only does she treat as an equal, but whose maid, Minnie, is surprised at Miss Celia's attitude, as no white lady ever treats a 'colored' maid this well.  Another character who ends up being likeable is Skeeter's mother, who is constantly trying to teach her daughter to behave lady-like, constantly nagging and telling her to wear this, do this, do that, and constantly manages to irritate Skeeter. The next character is Elizabeth Leefolt, a white who is not as rich as the society ladies of her times, but who is doing all she can to make it up. She is in awe of Hilly, her best friend, and ends up doing things the way Hilly says or wants. When faced with a tricky situation, she goes by what Hilly tells her to do, not by her own instinct. We also see her complete dependence on her 'colored' maid Aibileen, who she leaves to take charge of her infant entirely, but for whom she gets a separate toilet made out in the garage, just because Hilly didn't approve of the colored maid and the guests using the same toilet.<br /><br /> The book is a MUST-READ for anyone who would like to read a narration that is set in an important historical era, an issue that stirred up the world and that was one of the biggest causes of the past, and sometimes, even today. Though it is a work of fiction, the author has borrowed instances from many real-life events. At the end of the book, she gives us an insight into her own life, how she and her siblings were brought up by a colored help, and how she never thanked her for it. She says she too had behaved like many of these white women in her own life as a child, not giving much thought to her colored help, and is now truly ashamed of her thoughtlessness. The book will make many of us take another look at the way we treat our helps, at the way we treat those in society who are at lower rungs, and many times, treated poorly because of that.<br /><br /> Kathryn Stockett has given us a winner. Of course the movie received many international awards and nominations, but the book is any day much much more and much more detailed. A movie can never have the time and the logistics to completely capture the book's every detail, and it is because of this that I would recommend that you definitely read the book, even though you may have seen the movie.

B0015DWJX2|5.0|On the surface, this book is cheerful, light-hearted, and delightful.  Underneath, it gently and subtly explores important themes about loyalty, friendship, and love.  And it also touches on the gift of reading, which always warms my heart.<br /><br />The book is comprised entirely of letters written either by or to the main character, Juliet Ashton.  Juliet is a resident of London, who spent her days during World War II writing humorous newspaper articles documenting the war under a pseudonym by day and fighting to protect her city, especially its beloved books, from German bombs at night.  Now Juliet is looking for a new topic to write about when she stumbles upon the Guernsey Literary and Potato Peel Pie Society.  Guernsey is one of the English channel islands that were occupied by the Germans during the war, and Juliet soon gets to know the members of the literary society through letters they exchange.  While they discuss the topic of how reading helped the society members survive the pain of occupation, they also discuss how they cared for each other, each complete with their fears and flaws.  I am surprised to find myself tearing up as a write this, but I guess I shouldn't be surprised.  This book really touched my heart.  And it taught me a little something new about World War II, England, and human decency as well.<br /><br />This book doesn't have page-turning thrills.  Despite the war theme, there are no graphic descriptions of blood and gore.  Instead, it has something much better . . . people becoming their best under the worst of circumstances.

B006UG90RM|3.0|With initial impressions of the much anticipated new Van Halen album A Different Kind Of Truth leaving me with a fair degree of disappointment I wanted to let this one sit with me for a while before reviewing it, but I've now reached the point where I believe that I've given it enough listens (plenty... believe me!) to pass judgement on it.  Could this album have ever been as good as the hype surrounding it and reached the dizzy heights of the expectations of legions of fans around the world?  In reality probably not, and the truth is that for all the hype and expectations it isn't that great, but to be fair it isn't that bad either.<br /><br />After a decade of continual rumours of deals made and broken, David Lee Roth was finally confirmed as being back in the band for a new album, and EVH was reported to be healthy with alcohol & dug issues resolved.  So I couldn't help but think they would pick right up from where they left off with 1984 (my second favourite VH album) but perhaps with some of the best traits of the Van Hagar years infused.  Yeah, I had high expectations.  But surprisingly (to me anyway, perhaps not so to others) they have instead released an album which has far more in common with their first four albums.  All traces of Van Hagar have been expunged for what instead is a return to what could be considered a very retro album.  So much so in fact, that this album could have sat comfortably in 1982 as the album after Fair Warning.<br /><br />There has been a lot of negative comments made in on-line reviews about Diamond Dave's singing on this, how his voice has deteriorated, he's not the singer he once was, blah, blah, blah...  The truth as I see it is that he sounds just fine to me.  Yeah the high pitched \\"wow's\\" of the first few albums are absent, but they were always a little overdone anyway and really belong in the past.  Yeah there are more spoken vocal lines here than on previous Roth albums, but I think overall he really is in fine form here, and if this album isn't what you wanted it to be then the blame doesn't lie with him.<br /><br />I did wonder how they would go without their special hot sauce, Michael Anthony but the truth with that is that whilst the absence of his unique high vocals is noticeable, it doesn't make that much of a difference really.  Whoever they have got doing backing vocals do a reasonable job, and again if this album isn't what you wanted it to be then the blame doesn't lie there either.  Nor does it lie with the playing of the VH brothers or VH Jnr on bass.<br /><br />Where this album disappoints is in the songs themselves.  One would think (and expect) that in the fourteen years (yes 14!!!) between studio albums, EVH would have written enough songs that he would be able to select only the best material for what can only be described as their \\"come back\\" album.  So the fact that they have chosen to recycle some songs that they discarded in the late 1970's is initially quite a surprise.  But then when you think what they were aiming to do, i.e. release an album that does sound like their early years, it's actually probably the best way to get that sound.<br /><br />The trouble with never (pun intended) releasing them until now just confirms though why some of them were discarded in the first place.  Then there's the difficult task of making new songs that sound like their old ones did.  So whilst one might initially think that releasing an album like this is a cop out, I actually think it's a bold move, and I think they have pulled it off fairly successfully.  Why you ask when some of the songs aren't that great?  Well the real truth is that for all the brilliance on the early Van Halen albums and despite their immense popularity and likeability, they also contain what can only be described as some crap too.  So in that regard A Different Kind Of Truth does sit right alongside them, but probably more comfortably with Women And Children First and Fair Warning than VH1 and VH2 asthe latter named (but earlier released) were stronger as a whole.<br /><br />Honeybabysweetidoll is excrement in its purest form and Bullethead isn't much better, and China Town isn't much of a song either.  The Trouble With Never and Beats Workin' are ok songs and not shite by any means, but still average fare really.  Album opener Tattoo doesn't kick things off with a bang like you would expect and is an unusual choice for first single, so this hit me in the guts when I heard it and considerably lowered my expectations of what was gonna come.  Thankfully it isn't the best song on the album, but even so it has grown on me quite a bit so I rate it better than those mentioned above.  Stay Frosty isn't too bad and like many have mentioned, the comparisons to Icecream Man are obvious, but it isn't as good as that song.  Big River is nothing brilliant but still quite enjoyable nonetheless.<br /><br />But the good songs on this album are quite good and are my preferred tracks (in order):<br />Blood And Fire, You And Your Blues, She's The Woman, Outta Space, As Is.  It's just a shame that the rest aren't this good.the latter named (but earlier released) were stronger as a whole.<br /><br />Honeybabysweetidoll is excrement in its purest form and Bullethead isn't much better, and China Town isn't much of a song either. 1401302378|5.0|With 6 years of hindsight, the author's analysis of the broad impact of the long-tail of consumer demand unleashed by innovations in production, delivery and search, is clearly right-on.  The trends Anerson spotted have continued, in media and other industries, for the reasons he described. None of this is rocket science.  The Pareto Principle (80/20) is a stalwart of managerial finance and operations management curricula and real world applications and decisions.  However, the lowering of media costs means that the activity in the last 20% of the tail is where profitable, non-obvious opportunities lie.  The author does overreach a little at times, but these are not essential to the book.  He does repeat the same application or principle, but usually because it fits in the flow of another argument.  I thought that the economics was solid and that this was and is a more important long-run contribution than Freakonomics or the Tipping Point, although the former was more fun and diverse and the latter provided a broadly applicable new idea that was not so well known.

B006PAO2FS|5.0|Paul Mccartney's new album is beautiful. The album contains a series of ballads from the American songbook. This has been done by artists like Rod Stewart but the key difference with this work is in the arrangement. The instrumental backing is spare and of extremely high quality. There are no large orchestral moments drowning good songs and Mccartney's vocals are also placed really forward or high in the mix. This old time jazz trio style works perfectly for the material and is something very new in Mccartney's catalogue. In the past, I always felt great mccartney albums would have been better with his vocals louder. In this work, you hear every nuance of his voice and maybe that has put off some of the negative reviewers. This is a great relaxing late night album for fans of \\"and I love her\\" or \\"yesterday\\" type Beatle numbers.

B0025KVLTC|5.0|In looking at the Beatles' discography, SGT. PEPPER is often adored for its futuristic sonics and the sort of psychedelia that captures an era. But such an emphasis on SGT. PEPPER unfairly marginalizes their preceding record, for 1966's REVOLVER marks the true leap into psychedelia. If you think that this album still a transitional effort away from vacuous love ballads, consider this: the track \\"Tomorrow Never Knows\\", with its trippy tape loops, artificially generated seagull sounds, and John Lennon repeating Timothy Leary's advice to \\"Turn off your mind, relax and float downstream\\", was the *first* song put down at the REVOLVER sessions.<br /><br />That fact rather changes one's view of the album, doesn't it? George Harrison's two contributions are highly mature, with \\"Taxman\\" a sarcastic view of Britain's revenue service, and \\"Love You To\\" a droning sitar tune that is just as otherworldly as \\"Within You, Without You\\" on SGT. PEPPER. And could there be a more overt 1960s drug song than \\"Dr. Robert\\"? Even the most traditional tracks on this record, whether love songs or the silly \\"Yellow Submarine\\", turn out to be more than meets the eye. Reading [[ASIN:0754605574 Every Sound There Is]], a collection of papers on REVOLVER by a number of prominent musicologists, drew my attention to the innovative recording techniques used on this album.<br /><br />As a listener keenly interested in 1960s psychedelia, I've found REVOLVER to be among the most addictive and rewarding of the Beatles' LPs.

B005LAIHGI|4.0|After establishing himself as a satirical force with Da Ali G Show, Sacha Baron Cohen took his style of free-form, guerrilla comedy to the big screen with the brilliant Borat, a film that turned him into something of a cultural icon. The film was sharp, topical, and unpredictable in a way many spoofs simply can't replicate. Keeping up that level of success proved to be harder than many thought, as Cohen followed it up with the terribly unfunny supermodel film, Bruno, that seemed to stumble in every way that Borat got right. The Dictator, while marketed as being a sort of spiritual and creative cousin to Borat, is a total departure for Cohen, as it relies heavily on traditional Hollywood storytelling and jettisons the unscripted stuff we've become so accustomed to. The change hasn't effected Cohen in the least, as The Dictator is just as incendiary and depraved as we all hoped it would be.<br /><br />That's not to say the film has the insight of Borat, but it does show Cohen's deft ability to bring pointed, serious issues to light while hiding them under a thin veil of gross out gags, penis humor, and stunning political incorrectness. The firebrand of a closing monologue is a thing of genius, even if the message he's sending is an obvious and disturbing one. But that's getting ahead just a little bit, because there are an incredible amount of laughs to be had before reaching that point. Cohen plays General Aladeen, a hilarious Saddam Hussein knock-off who runs the north African nation of Wadiya with an iron fist. Born into power and demanding of his people's support, he's both vengeful and extremely petty, powered by years of privilege. He has the money to do basically anything he wants, like nail some of the hottest women in Hollywood. You either worship him as the leader or....well, the other option is pretty nasty. When the UN comes calling with claims of Wadiya fostering a nuclear program, Aladeen jets to New York with his security detail to try and head off any possible sanctions. When he arrives, he's naturally floored that our country is as free as it appears, while also noting some of the obvious hypocrisies. Not long after arriving, he's betrayed by his elder brother(Sir Ben Kingsley), who has him kidnapped and replaced by a moronic body double who will sign a UN agreement making Wadiya a true democracy. Why? So that Big Oil execs, all named after their corporate masters, can swoop in and buy up the oil fields for a hefty sum.<br /><br />Presumed dead and shaved of his beard, the symbol of his rightful power, Aladeen is forced to try and acclimate to American culture while finding a way to stop the signing. The dictatorship must be protected at all costs! He becomes reluctant friends with a political activist named Zoey(Anna Faris), a vegan and a woman (*gasp*) who takes him in and gives him a job, even as he insults and demeans her right to her face. In fact, jokes about the perceived inferiority and unworthiness of females are probably the most prevalent of all in this film, although no group is spared Cohen's wrath. For once, we're treated to a spirited comedy that hasn't had all of the best gags ruined by trailers, which is a remarkable feat considering all that we've seen of the movie. Smartly, Cohen has chosen to use his gift for creating spontaneous controversy as a means of hyping the film, such as turning up at the Academy Awards as Aladeen, then promptly spilling ashes all over Ryan Seacrest.<br /><br />Cohen is a master at eliciting uncomfortable laughter from situations we know are just plain wrong! When Aladeen and his reluctant compatriot, a scientist named Nadal(the perfectly cast Jason Mantzoukas), turn up at a funeral for an African-American man with hopes of stealing his epic beard, it's impossible not to hide your face at the racial firestorm that's about to be unleashed. No subject is taboo, and even the 9/11 tragedy is used to clever, comical effect. It's at times like this when Cohen is at his most dangerous, and it seems to sharpen his edge in a way. There's no doubt that many people are going to be turned off by this scene in particular, and it's hard to blame them. It's equally difficult not to respect Cohen's brazenness to go where nobody else would dare to. There are times when the film seems to stretch pretty hard to keep up the steady stream of gags, and it's at times like this when you wish Cohen would depart from the script and just go out and insult some ignorant bystander. Like most of Cohen's creations, Aladeen isn't a character you necessarily root for. He's more like somebody you love to hate. That said, he's as memorable and quotable as Borat was, and certainly less irritating than Bruno.<br /><br />It helps that Cohen gets a ton of support from a fantastic supporting cast, another reason why this film succeeds where some of his improvised efforts haven't.  Mantzoukas is brilliant as Aladeen's accomplice, Nadal, a scientist who the dictator thought he had executed. Nadal is just the right prism from which to view Aladeen's character, a guy who has loyalty to the system Aladeen represents, but also has a good deal of hatred for the man himself. He also knows the true nature of Aladeen's heart, and manipulates him at every turn. Anna Faris is basically relegated to being the focal point for Cohen's verbal jabs(he calls her a \\"lesbian hobbit\\"), and it takes some getting used to seeing her held back in such a way. A number of priceless celebrity cameos also swell the film's ranks, but they are also best left unspoiled. The most surprising scene stealer of all had to be MadTV's Bobby Lee as an ambassador who likes to exert his power in a very special kind of way. Again, something best left for you to discover yourself, but let's just say it involves some very interesting folks doing something unexpected.<br /><br />As Cohen has branched off into more legitimate roles, such as co-starring in Martin Scorsese's Hugo, it's going to be interesting to see if it leads to a softening of his style. For now, The Dictator proves again that nobody is as outrageously offensive as Cohen, and we wouldn't have it any other way. \\"Jachnun\\"!!t dangerous, and it seems to sharpen his edge in a way. B005LAIHIQ|2.0|They're creepy and they're kooky, mysterious and...oh, that's another crazy horror TV family. Dark Shadows has managed to achieve some measure of cult status thanks to its quirky, macabre premise, more so than for any claim to being good. It was unique at least, as fairly serious goth soap operas featuring monsters and parallel universes are kinda hard to come by nowadays. You won't have to look hard to find the yearly Dark Shadows Festival, and the show has been attempted as a remake on more than one occasion. They all failed. Let's face it, remaking a series that seems to defy typical genre conventions is a tough act. Since most people have no clue what the heck Dark Shadows was, and a TV series is a proven failure, what's the best way to make use of this niche property? Make a movie out of it.<br /><br />Unfortunately that seems to be the only consideration at play in this tired and disappointing version of Dark Shadows, made even worse by the presence of Tim Burton and Johnny Depp, who on paper would seem to be the perfect fit. Over the years, Burton has strayed further away from original material, shutting off much of his creative genius in order to bring to life the vision of others, and this would be the best evidence that all those years away have caused him to lose his touch. The same goes for Depp, who in his eighth collaboration(!!!) with Burton has fallen into the same comfortable formula as him. Burton appears perfectly happy to apply his talents to substandard, franchise friendly material. Depp, who has spent more time playing goofball characters than actual human beings, has reached the point where it looks as if he's not even trying anymore.<br /><br />This Dark Shadows bears many of the hallmarks of the original series, Burton-ized for maximum marketability. That's not to say that making an obviously mainstream film is a bad thing. It's just that what made Burton so special is that he could do it without it ever feeling like he was compromising his artistic vision. Those days, sadly, are gone, traded in for half-hearted love triangles and lazy culture clash jokes sprinkled with spiffy 1970s rock tunes. The film begins promisingly in 1760, where the Collins clan have uprooted themselves and moved to Maine, where they began a thriving seafood company that powers the entire town's economy. They're so beloved that the town actually becomes known as Collinsport, but that's about where all the good cheer ends. Young Barnabas Collins(Depp) has become the object of desire of their servant girl, Angelique(Eva Green), and he has given in to passion with her on numerous occasions, even though he has found true love with the beautiful Josette(Bella Heathcote). When Barnabas tries to break things off with Angelique, she gets revenge by casting a murderous spell on Josette, destroying the Collins family, and cursing Barnabas as a vampire. Oh, and then she has him buried alive.<br /><br />Fast forward a couple of hundred years to 1972, and the Collins family is still feeling the effects of Angelique's curse. The once stately Collins Manor has fallen into disarray, broken and disheveled. The family business is in tatters, with the surviving Angelique using the years to take over the seafood industry for herself, sending the Collins' into ruin. All that remains of the family are a handful of heirs with secrets and quirks galore: the regal matriarch, Elizabeth Collins Stoddard(Michelle Pfeiffer); her insufferable, gold-digging brother(Johnny Lee Miller); the rebellious teenage daughter, Carolyn(Chloe Moretz); and the troubled 10-year old son, David(Gully McGrath). Things begin to turn around with the arrival of Victoria Winters(Heathcote again) as the new governess, who happens to be the spitting image of Josette. When Barnabas is accidentally unearthed from his grave(and promptly slaughters everyone nearby), he rejoins the family and sets out to restore them to their greater glory.<br /><br />Of course it won't be easy, especially when Barnabas quickly falls for Victoria, and she seems drawn mysteriously to him. Like a replay from their youth, Angelique jealously does everything in her power to steal Barnabas away, inciting a rivalry between her and the Collins clan that threatens to consume the town. What could have been a hilarious and spirited war between supernatural foes, mixed with Burton's freakish flair for the dramatic, turns out to be a one-note affair. Much of the humor derives for Barnabas's inability to adapt to modern times, spazzing out over the \\"tiny songstress\\" singing on the television. He's generally lost in the cultural freedom of the time period, although he seems to have the \\"free love\\" ideal down pretty well. Burton, along with horror novelist turned screenwriter Seth Grahame-Smith(Abraham Lincoln: Vampire Hunter), mine the fish out of water humor for all it's worth. They're actually quite funny for awhile, but like most of Depp's impersonations they wear thin really quick. It doesn't help that the rest of the Collins clan are basically ignored, seen only when reacting to some misstep Barnabas has made. Moretz, a fantastic and mature young actress is badly miscast as Carolyn, a vampy bit of jailbait with a snotty attitude and great \\"birthing hips\\". Not even Helena Bonham Carter, such a staple and a highlight of many Burton movies, has much to do here as the family psychiatrist, Dr. Julia Hoffman. The most interesting thing about her, other than the momentary \\"fling\\" she has with the over-sexed Barnabas, is her blazing scarlet hair. Only Eva Green's busty and sinful performance as Angelique has any hint of life to it. She can curse me anytime she wants. We just aren't given much of a reason to care about anybody, and that includes Barnabas.<br /><br />The fundamental problem is the inability to blend the show's campy, soap opera elements with Burton's rather specific sort of black comedy. The tone is all over the map, from dark and rather vicious, to downright silly. Sometimes this can be a good thing, and Burton has shown many times he can usually find a way to make these elements work in perfect harmony. He never pulls it off here, though, and a big part of the problem is Graham-Smith's haphazard writing. Having read both of his popular, genre mash-up novels, that inconsistency is a recurring theme. He's a writer with a lot of cool ideas but little clue how to pull them off. That fits what happens to Dark Shadows to a tee. There's so much potential here, from the concept to the cast, that it's aggravating to sit and watch it go absolutely nowhere. At least it looks like a Burton film. He certainly hasn't lost his touch for art and production design.<br /><br />Burton and Depp have stated in the past how they were huge fans of theTV series, and it's mainly due to them that this film exists. They must've only really enjoyed the occasional episode or two, because it quickly becomes apparent that they have no idea where a Dark Shadows movie should lead. Clocking in at a ponderous 113 minutes, you start to feel every second of it, and the ending indicates the possibility of sequels. Rather than going in that direction, maybe Burton and Depp should just get together and pop in the Blu-Rays for a weekend marathon.ally when Barnabas quickly falls for Victoria, and she seems drawn mysteriously to him. They must've only really enjoyed the occasional episode or two, because it quickly becomes apparent that they have no idea where a Dark Shadows movie should lead. Clocking in at a ponderous 113 minutes, you start to feel every second of it, and the ending indicates the possibility of sequels. Rather than going in that direction, maybe Burton and Depp should just get together and pop in the Blu-Rays for a weekend marathon.

0345803485|2.0|I'm not sure exactly why I'm adding this review to be buried amongst the thousands, but I gave this book a lot of thought as I read it, so why not?<br /><br />Through most of the book, I considered giving it a 3-star review.  I didn't expect to love it, but found the story kept my interest and flowed well enough.  The end, however, changed my perspective enough to drop it down to 2.  Throughout most of the book, I felt Christian was a manipulator of the first order - sure he had a tough life, and I know the following books (which I will not read) are supposed to address it further.  No, Christian is an abuser, needs very serious help, and Ana should run, not walk, out of his life for good here.<br /><br />And for the record, I knew before I started that while some of the sex acts are BDSM, and I suppose Christian would like to think it's his lifestyle, but in fact it is not.  I suppose it should make the scenes more titillating, but it didn't.  Still, I would have given three...<br /><br />As for the character development up to this point... well, having not read Twilight (though I have read a couple of other Twilight Fanfictions), I now have a perfect understanding of \\"How Twilight Works\\" on \\"The Oatmeal.\\"  Ana is an empty shell who doesn't appreciate who she is or what she has.  Then we are repeatedly reminded of \\"Edward's perfect face,\\" oh sorry, I meant Christian Grey's perfect grey eyes.  They fall madly, instantly in love from the moment Ana falls into his office (even though he doesn't realize it for what it is yet).  He then stalks, overwhelms and seduces her, but that's okay... well until that final scene, apparently.<br /><br />Despite all that, I still was compelled to continue reading. The story did hold my interest, possibly more than that, but in the same sense as watching reality TV or listening to Howard Stern - just to see what's going to happen next.  Hence the almost considered 3-star rating.<br /><br />What bothered me most as I read, at least upuntil the final scene (I may be beating a dead horse here, let's just take that as a given from here on), was the fact that this book, distributed by a subsidiary of Doubleday, really - desperately - needed an editor.<br /><br />Yes, I am going to pick on the grammar, but then I do a fair bit of grammar editing for independent authors.  I could go on about the simple sentence structure and repetitiveness, but I'll go straight for my personal favorite.  Someone needs to learn how to use commas.  I know it's petty, but it really bugs me when a sentence (I wish I'd bookmarked it - it was in the first 15% of the book) not only doesn't make sense because the missing comma makes the sentence say exactly the opposite of what I'm sure the author intended, given the context).  I did bookmark: \\"He smells, clean, fresh, heavenly...\\"<br /><br />On the other hand, I don't claim to be a great content editor, and even cutting some slack for the author not being from the United States, and maybe I can allow that since her children are not (or were not at writing) at University age, she might not realize much about the lives of young adults in 2011.  An editor should have though.<br /><br />I know others have commented, a college student without a computer?  And no email?  And I have to agree.  Personal computers are practically required, and if nothing else, the school would issue an email address, because that's how their primary form of admin-faculty-student communication.<br /><br />Okay, but maybe she couldn't afford $500 for an average Windows laptop at any time during her time at school... But if that's the case, how could she possibly afford to book a flight across the country on the day of travel?  And when she actually does travel, she secretly leaves her Blackberry on, and expects to receive email on it while in flight?  Telephone data plans don't work that way.<br /><br />Similarly, though not on the same level, when she's upset one night after Christian leaves, well after 10, she decides to call her mother in Georgia.  I can deal with that actually, but shouldn't she at least be concerned about it being 2AM in Georgia?  And shouldn't her mother sound like she just woke up?<br /><br />So clearly, there were a lot of things which annoyed me, but I kept reading, I kept thinking I'll be kind and give it 3 stars for exceeding my expectations.  Now I feel generous giving it two.

0393088774|4.0|Paul Krugman's new book, End this Depression Now, paints the reality of Depression II with the colors of truth and the promise of a solution. Anyone concerned about current politics, jobs and our future, no matter the political persuasion, had better read this one. A Nobel Prize winner in Economics, Paul knows whereof he speaks ---a must read.<br /><br />Dr. Krugman makes clear that indeed we are in a depression. He defines our present circumstances as a liquidity trap in which the majority of the population cuts back on spending in order to reduce debt. Businesses do the same; they lay off workers and build up a reserve. Banks too deleverage to ride out the storm, but get right back into the shadow-banking, derivatives and chinagins that helped get us into this mess. (J.P. Morgan just lost two billion through misdirected dark derivative trading.) Consumers will not borrow even at zero percent and interest rates can't go much lower than that. Like Japan for a decade, we cannot grow out of the liquidity trap.<br /><br />Krugman goes to great lengths to describe the polarization of economists in their conflicting understanding or misunderstanding of the causes and solutions to our dilemma. Economists apparently understood the causes of the Great Depression, and legislators as well enacted regulations and safeguards to prevent it happening again. Since the 1980s, however, legislators supported newer, more fashionable and erroneous economic beliefs, systematically repealing those earlier safeguards. I like Krugman's description of current economists as: salt water economists versus freshwater economists. Those may be euphemisms for Democrats and Republicans. In any case Salt Water Economists appear to be the good guys.<br /><br />Quoting from the book, \\"Yet Lucas, a Nobel laureate who was a towering, almost dominate figure in macroeconomics for much of the 1970s and 1980s, wasn't wrong in saying that economists had learned a lot since the 1930s. By, say, 1970 the economics profession really did know enough to prevent a recurrence of anything resembling the Great Depression. --- And then much of the profession proceeded to forget what it had learned.\\" Krugman goes on to explain, \\"How a mix of politics and runaway academic sociology, through which basically absurd notions, became dogma in analysis of both finance and macroeconomics.\\"<br /><br />I love the phrase, \\"runaway academic sociology.\\" I take it as a foreshadowing of the trade deficit and the funneling of middle class wealth into the hands of the wealthy. I call it a money pump that drained wealth from the Middle Class, first to our trading partners and then back in the form of foreign investment into the hands of the nouveau riche plutocracy that so benefited from this flow of wealth, the 1% and the 0.01%. I thought - and I was mistaken - that Krugman would define academic-sociology as the near messianic belief that unregulated trade could do no wrong.<br /><br />Krugman makes a compelling case for using the tools that we still have to end this depression. He argues soundly that the stimulus package did not go far enough. His suggestions listed first of all government-spending as the essential ingredient for getting us out of the liquidity trap.  The list includes areas of immediate impact like funding states and local governments at such a level as to meet the need for projected but canceled spending on critical local measures -- such as teachers. The list further includes spending on critical infrastructure, not exotics, but things like the electric grid, rail-bed upgrades, roads, bridges and so forth. Interestingly, Krugman shows that a measured degree of inflation at around 4% could further stimulate production reduce the cost of debt and allow for a real interest rate below 0%. (The real cost of money must include inflation) Furthermore, Krugman establishes, at least to my satisfaction that inflation will not come roaring back. He additionally favors more and extended relief for those who struggle, and he further proposes mortgage payment relief through refinancing. He also mentioned the need to push China to follow the rules about the value of its currency.<br /><br />Krugman attributes the depression to the systematic dismantling of the banking rules which had been designed to prevent the depression from recurring and the aggressive behavior of banks and other businesses as a result of loosening those safeguards.<br /><br />I did not find any description of the role that the trade deficit did or did not play in this crisis. Last month's deficit in the balance-of-trade came in at over fifty billion dollars. The trade deficit just keeps growing -- and for many decades now. I do not know how you can explain the depression without acknowledging the not so slow drain on the US consumer from jobs, manufacturing and sales revenue going overseas. I do not see in Krugman's book, the big money flow that makes the 1 - 0.1% so wealthy and the middle class so poor. We all know that for generations now our wives have had to work. We also know the struggle to pay college tuition for our children, many if not most families deferring to college loans - but that's another story. Politically incorrect as it may be, the trade deficit looms as the unmentioned elephant in the livingroom. Six hundred billion a year amounts to over 4% of the GDP. Anyway we look at it that's a drain. Furthermore most of it returns in the form of foreign investment seeking a safe haven. It must keep hedge fund managers staying up at night thinking up investment instruments to sop up that transferred wealth. I don't see numbers equating the loss to the middle class by service jobs transfered overseas, or the loss of local jobs as a result of manufacturing overseas.<br /><br />American companies' overseas profits remain overseas because of high corporate tax rates. Furthermore, domestic corporations reduce their tax burden by paying upper management inflated salaries. The executives manage tax shelters by their own means, thus paying less tax than the average middleclass consumer. So, the Middle Class not only unwittingly transfers wealth to the wealthy, but pays their share of the taxes on it as well.<br />Let's end this trade deficit now and end the money pump. Daddy Warbucks will scream his head off and pay a fortune in PR convincing the public that you can't do that. He is laughing all the way to the bank - he is the bank. Krugman's book stimulates these thoughts and questions in my mind. I hope more of the American public will ask these questions as well.<br /><br />My economics was at Kansas. Not being too clear on Keynesian economics, I looked up my college professor's opinion of Keynesian theory. In John Ise's introduction to his 1950 text, Economics, He writes, \\"Although I do not regard Keynesian doctrine as the whole of the `new economics,` I have injected Keynesian theory at various points in the book, as well as in the new chapter.\\"<br /><br />Apparently, not all fresh water economists were anti  Keynes, or was Kansas the exception with no water, just sod and wheat?elief for those who struggle, and he further proposes mortgage payment relief through refinancing. He also mentioned the need to push China to follow the rules about the value of its currency.<br /><br />Krugman attributes the depression to the systematic dismantling of the banking rules which had been designed to prevent the depression from recurring and the aggressive behavior of banks and other businesses as a result of loosening those safeguards.<br /><br />I did not find any description of the role that the trade deficit did or did not play in this crisis. I'm an English instructor, and I have ideation, intellection, learner, strategic, activator, input, and maximizer. Perfect! (Gallup tells you the order of all 34 strengths after you take some of their advanced classes. This has been helpful as well.) Knowing my students' strengths helps me know how to approach them, motivate them, and reminds me not to underestimate them. An apathetic-looking student might have \\"achiever\\" and just not know how to use it yet. Maybe he/she uses it in the videogame world, but doesn't think there's a good application of it in real life. \\"Adaptability\\" people need frequent change in their tasks. \\"WOO\\" students want you to enjoy their charm, and will work harder if you do. Learners are naturals in the classroom. \\"Harmony\\" people hate conflict. \\"Restorative\\" people will learn well with problem-solving activities. Etc. The strengths tools have reinvigorated my teaching approach. They've also given my husband and me great insight into how different we are, and why it's so important to give each other space to be who we are. So, it's beneficial at many levels. In other words: This is a valuable tool. Find out your top-five, but then dig in more deeply, so that you get a real understanding of what the results mean.

B007MDB71O|5.0|1977 was a transformative year in my life.  It started with the discovery of my first \\"Conan the Barbarian\\" comic book at the Eckerd drug store near my house.  Little did I know at the time that I had just crossed the threshold into a whole new world of endless wonders.  This discovery soon led me to the \\"John Carter: Warlord of Mars\\" comic that Marvel was publishing at the time, and in a short couple of months I was immersed in the amazing artwork of Frank Frazetta, as well as the original pulp stories and novels by authors like Robert E. Howard, and of course, Edgar Rice Burroughs.  1977 also marked the premiere of a little known film at the time called \\"Star Wars\\".  I have often said over the last 35 years that the first time I saw \\"Star Wars\\" was the single best time I've ever had at the movies, and while there have certainly been a lot of great films in the last three decades, I was convinced that movies simply didn't have the power to make me feel like that again.  I was wrong.  \\"John Carter\\" managed to do what I thought was impossible: make me feel like that wide-eyed 11 year old kid again.  It goes without saying that I've been waiting for this film for 35 years, but it was worth the wait.  \\"John Carter\\" exceeded - no, shattered - my expectations.<br /><br />The original \\"Princess of Mars\\" is one of my favorite novels of all time.  I've read it so many times over the last three decades that I've lost count, and while some Edgar Rice Burroughs fans were disappointed or even outraged at the deviations from the original source material, in my humble opinion the changes were in most of not all cases justified.  Bob Clampett, the first person who tried to get a John Carter film off the ground in the 1930s, said this about his meetings with Burroughs: \\"Edgar was smart enough to understand that one couldn't just literally translate his Mars books page by page into animation; it just would not be cinematic. So, he gave me a great deal of freedom to dream up and be inspired by his writing and develop a cartoon story on my own.\\"  While ERB was a tremendously gifted author, his works aren't without flaws, and they're a bit dated; there's very little moral ambiguity, he often recycles his own plot twists, and his plots often rely on coincidence.  Additionally, ERB's heroes tend to be sort of Mary Sue characters with no visible physical or moral flaws. I can't see how John Carter or Ulysses Paxton or Cathoris or Gahan would behave differently in the similar circumstances.  So I can see why Stanton chose to give John Carter some personal angst and some character development in order to sell him to modern audiences.  Some have argued that Stanton's changes have rendered a character unrecognizable to fans of the original novels, but I would ask them \\"what characteristics define John Carter as a character?\\"  There's the physical characteristics: black hair (though it's closely cropped in the original novels...no biggie), heightened strength and agility, fairly tall, good looking, in excellent physical health, and agelessness. Although the last characteristic was not overtly stated in the film, it was highly suggested, if people pay attention to the subtle clues in the film. How could Carter, a man who appears to be about the age of thirty (as in the novel) possibly have been able to hold ERB on his knee when ERB was a child and tell him about his wild adventures all over the world? Was Carter doing this as a teenager? And then there's the fact that Carter doesn't age at all during the ten bitter years exiled back on Earth, when the effects wizards could easily have accomplished this with some rudimentary makeup effects.<br /><br />Then there's Carter's personality: Fearless, willing to throw himself in harm's way against impossible odds; selfless, willing to lay down his life for others; Carter behaves consistent with these characteristics throughout the film, even while he's giving lip service to \\"only caring about his cave of gold\\".He rescues Powell from the Apaches when he could have just left him to die; when he sees the air battle between Zodanga and Helium, he expresses his innate sense of justice and fairness with the remark \\"That don't look like a fair fight!\\" and then he throws himself into the thick of the battle when he spots Dejah (whom he doesn't even know) in imminent danger.<br /><br />Some argue that the dead wife and child back-story and Carter's initial reluctance to get involved transform Carter into a character unrecognizable to ERB fans, but I disagree.  I saw a character onscreen that was TRYING not to be noble and selfless because of a personal loss, but was failing at it, and I think the dichotomy enriched the story and drove home the values that were important to Burroughs.  This point is driven home by Dejah Thoris, who sees through the wall he's built around himself. My favorite line of the film: \\"We may be worlds apart, but I know you, John Carter. From the moment you caught me out of the sky, I knew. I felt the heart of a man willing to lay down his life for others\\". That fits ERB's character to a tee.  Without the back-story, that line would not have worked.<br /><br />...which brings us to the character changes to Dejah Thoris.  The main difference is the fact that this Dejah Thoris can actually fight, but I can't believe in this post Princess Leia, post Sarah Conner, post Ripley world that either ERB fans or film fans in general were expecting anything less, or would accept a Dejah Thoris who is physically helpless. This concept was outdated even in the late seventies when Marv Wolfman and Chris Clairemont were penning the \\"John Carter: Warlord of Mars\\" comics, and even then, having read the original novels during the same time period, I welcomed this enhancement. One has to remember that when \\"A Princess of Mars\\" was written in the early 20th Century, women were supposed to be prim and proper at all times, and \\"respectable\\" women rarely even worked out of the home. They didn't even have the right to vote. A woman who was basically helpless to the whims of the man who controlled her (either her father or husband) was the norm rather than the exception. Such outdated conventions would never work in a modern film, unless it was a period piece specifically made to highlight such inequality.<br /><br />The Therns are much more powerful in the film than in the original novels. But the Therns in Burroughs' novels are actually pretty weak when their secrets are exposed, and ERB never really went anywhere with them, other than to have their leader kidnap Dejah Thoris and flee from John Carter (continuously). Burroughs' original concept is compelling, but I think for the sake of drama one must make the \\"bad guys\\" much more powerful than the good guys so that they have a near insurmountable task to overcome. How are the Therns similar to ERBs? They're using a false religion as a global scam to exploit the entire civilization and keep themselves in power, so the subtext is pretty similar.<br /><br />Zodanga isn't even mentioned in \\"A Princess of Mars\\" until the last 5th of it, and this simply wouldn't work in a feature film.  But Burroughs original pro-environmental message remains intact.  Barsoom is a dying planet, its oceans have long since dried up, and Zodanga is selfishly consuming the lion's share of the dwindling resources and making war on its neighbors rather than cooperating with them to find a solution. It was Stanton and Chabon's way of making the theme contemporary while still acknowledging the original source material.<br /><br />In summary, I think that overall the film is a much more balanced and coherent narrative than the novel it's based on, and it's remarkably true to the spirit of it, certainly truer than any other ERB adaptation in the last century.  And the pacing and scope of the film, as well as Michael Giacchino's magnificent score (that simultaneously manages to channel both John Williams and Basil Poledouris at their best - seriously, this is one of the best soundtracks of all time) bring to mind the classic films of the 70s and 80's, the kinds of films they don't make anymore but you wish they did, like \\"Star Wars\\" and \\"Superman\\" and \\"Raiders of the Lost Ark\\".  I loved all the actors in their respective roles, and thought Taylor Kitsch was near perfect as John Carter, but the real gem in this film is Lynn Collins' performance as Dejah Thoris. I was initially skeptical when I found out she had been cast as \\"the most beautiful woman on two worlds\\", but she OWNED the role, both physically and performance wise.  She IS Dejah Thoris.<br /><br />I simply can't recommend this film enough.  After my first viewing, I was numb; on my second, I soaked in and savored every frame of it.  On the third and subsequent viewings, I literally wept at how good it was. The only thing preventing this film from being considered a classic is the fact that not enough people have seen it yet, but that situation will change.  Mark my words: like \\"Blade Runner\\" and other films that initially met with a cool reception, the decades are going to be kind to \\"John Carter\\".

For example, the \\"Sea of Monsters\\" storyboard sequence from 1999 has the storyboard sequence running at the top, with the film clip at the bottom.  On the 2012 edition, the two segments run side-by-side in little boxes, which are hard to see.<br /><br />Pencil drawings: The 1999 version has 29 drawings, the 2012 version has only 26 (the three Hey Bulldog cels are missing). Has the same presentation problems as the storyboard sequence (full-size in 1999, smaller in 2012).<br /><br />Behind-the-scenes photos - There are 30 full-screen photos in the 1999 version, but only 29 in the 2012 version. Once again, the 2012 photos are smaller.<br /><br />Also, the 1999 version has a counter at the bottom center of the screen for the storyboards, photos, and pencil drawings.  The 2012 version does not, and it is very tedious to count them manually.<br /><br />Booklet:  The 2012 booklet has a new essay by John Lasseter of Walt Disney Studios, and reproduces all of the mini-essays from the 1999 version.  The booklet also has more artwork.  The package also comes with four mini-cels of the cartoon John, Paul, George, and Ringo, a sticker sheet of both the individual and collective cartoon Beatles, a Blue Meanie, the Flying Glove (\\"It's not polite to point!\\"), Jeremy Hillary Boob, the LOVE logo, and the Yellow Submarine itself.  There is also a promotional flyer for the [[ASIN:0763658545 Yellow Submarine: Midi Edition]] picture book and the LOVE Cirque du Soleil show in Las Vegas (this is Apple, so what do you expect?).<br /><br />Look for the 1999 version if you want the original non-DTS English 5.1 soundtrack, the \\"music-only\\" non-DTS 5.1 soundtrack, the Japanese subtitles, and a full-screen presentation of the storyboards, pencil drawings, and behind-the-scenes photos.  Otherwise, get the 2012 version. B001TH16DS|5.0|In a very real sense, this is the one I was 'saving up for'. I own all three remastered seasons of the original STAR TREK but only the first is on Blu-ray. Why? Well, I kind of wanted just one major treat (in its current best possible showcase) so, for me, it became a case of choosing which season had the most magic and created the most impact when I was very young. In the end, emotion won out to cold, hard logic...<br /><br />I love the fact that the first season is clearly finding it's feet (a problem for some fans, admittedly), that everyone involved is giving it absolutely everything they've got and, in the majority of episodes, all that effort really does show - the whole venture feels like a hugely exciting and entertaining step into the unknown. And, of course, the wonderful CGI. The second season, on the other hand, is a little more \\"Ain't we doin' good, guys?\\", a touch of ego, smugness and better restaurants, alongside the expected hike in confidence. As a consequence, it's a little less likeable. Only marginally, but still. The third season...well it tries hard, but it can't quite shake off a very real sense of fatigue and disappointment at the imposed budgetary contraints and a dismal network-scheduled timeslot.<br /><br />The first season on Blu-ray is also beautiful to look at; the acting, the dialogue, the lighting...it's all first rate and leaps off the screen at the senses. And that's why it (my decision) makes perfect sense. Just one on Blu-ray, the rest on DVD. No dilution of the magic and a major treat by any other name. Anyone who's read my other reviews on seasons two and three will know how I feel about Star Trek, and why it remains the best tv sci-fi ever.<br /><br />EXPECT THE UNEXPECTED - A FEW CHOICE MOMENTS...<br /><br />ERRAND OF MERCY<br />Refusing to divulge information regarding the Federation's military strength, Kirk instead tells his Klingon captor, Kor, to \\"Go climb a tree\\". Uhura cannot silence the comms and Kirk is furious, so he tells her off. She instantly returns with, \\"Don't you think I would if I could!\\" and the captain is suitably chastised. A pause, then he apologises, to which she smiles, instantly forgiving him. The issues of authority and friendship (and stress!) are beautifully played here, rendering the moment incredibly subtle and effective. These are the little things that make Star Trek special.<br /><br />THE GALILEO SEVEN<br />The extended laughter of the entire bridge crew as Spock acknowledges he is a stubborn sod moments before the end credits begin to roll. It's either a complete cheese-fest OR an inspired piece of character-enhancement. Yeah, well it's the latter, ok? Ever the optimist.<br /><br />THE MAN TRAP<br />A fatigued McCoy contacts the bridge and asks Kirk for advice on how to relax a bit. Kirk recommends the same tablets prescribed to him by the good doctor on a previous occasion (they must have worked but had really unpleasant side-effects as he's clearly still narked about them). \\"You'll sleep\\", he says, before abruptly cutting off the comms screen.<br /><br />WHAT ARE LITTLE GIRLS MADE OF?<br />Ruk is a seven-and-a-half foot tall android killing machine.
