B0017VG69U|5.0|A CLASSIC MAKEOVER, FROM MY AMAZON.CO.UK REVIEWS<br /><br />Excellent remastering of the original STAR TREK SEASON 2 source material. The new CGI Enterprise, planets and stars are seamlessly integrated and the 35mm episodes are given a vivid makeover. Those Starfleet 'red-shirts' have never looked more gloriously doomed (see a bunch of them drop like flies after being attacked by a sweet-smelling alien gas cloud in episode 13: OBSESSION). Fantastic stuff, and there's no doubt that the visual impact on display here would have been just what the creators had in mind over forty years ago, despite lacking the technology and the budget to make it a reality at the time. Has it been worth the wait, then? Absolutely...and yet, in the cutting-edge 21st century, there's still a tiny part of me that can't quite let go of the old 15ft long starship model (complete with rubbish matte lines) in orbit above the same old alien world - Alpha 177 - every other week. Nostalgia can be a double-edged sword.<br /><br />However, there are many great episodes in Season 2 which really benefit from the CGI treatment, including: AMOK TIME, THE DOOMSDAY MACHINE, THE IMMUNITY SYNDROME, JOURNEY TO BABEL and THE ULTIMATE COMPUTER.<br /><br />.........................<br /><br />SUSPENSION OF DISBELIEF: A FEW CHOICE MOMENTS REQUIRING THAT EXTRA BIT OF CONCENTRATION...<br /><br />Episode 3: THE CHANGELING<br />Uhura's had her mind wiped by a metre-tall space probe called Nomad. Fortunately, the Enterprise has the facilities to retrain and re-educate her. But surely that could take years? Not to mention dealing with the deep psychological trauma associated with such an incident. Apparently not. All you need in the 23rd century, it seems, is Nurse Chapel and a small library-computer screen. Soon be opening those hailing frequencies again, dear.<br />VERDICT: Utterly Amazing.<br /><br />Episode 10: JOURNEY TO BABEL<br />The Tellarite Ambassador, Gav, is an alien with anger-management issues. Hardly surprising given his badly-fitting mask, although it probably lent authenticity to the actor's performance. Clearly someone in the make-up department paying scant attention when that particular episode went before the cameras. Where's Roddenberry when you need him? (Given his penchant for the fairer sex in all their, er, IDIC, probably best not to ask.)<br />VERDICT: Painfully Obvious. And Obviously Painful.<br /><br />Episode 4: MIRROR MIRROR<br />This Parallel-Universe thing...no matter what your start point is, the simple action of stepping on a butterfly, so the theory goes, will cause events to change in the future. Paths that may have run along similar lines to begin with will eventually diverge. Given those circumstances, would the landing party and their evil counterparts REALLY be dealing with the Halkan Council at precisely the same moment in time to allow for a switch? Come on, what are the odds?<br />VERDICT: Somewhat Unlikely, I'd Say.<br /><br />EPISODE 26: ASSIGNMENT EARTH<br />Blatant attempt by Roddenberry to get an entirely different series off the ground. How can you tell? Easy, because Kirk and spock are given very little to do in the third act. And That Is Just Not On. I'd have called my agent long before the focus shifted towards the block of wood masquerading as Gary Seven and his annoyingly dopey 'assistant', Miss Roberta Lincoln. Bad call.<br />VERDICT: See How You Like Friday Nights At Ten, Buster.<br /><br />Episode 12: THE DEADLY YEARS<br />Typical Shatner, too vain to let Kirk suffer natural baldness during the story's rapid ageing mystery. We knew he wore a hairpiece even then but it's been bluff and denial ever since. Mind you, anyone who can emerge unscathed from a Comedy Central 'Roasting' has my everlasting respect.<br />VERDICT: If The Glue Sticks...<br /><br />Episode 4 (again!): MIRROR MIRROR<br />This moment doesn't strictly belong here, but being so unexpectedly cool I reckon it merits inclusion on any terms. Okay, here we go...as Kirk exits the turbolift on the 'mirror' Enterprise, the flat hand (not a fist, a hand) of an assassin thumps him on the face dead centre. A split second later the hand pulls away to reveal a bloodied lip and a disoriented Captain. Yes, there's a rather more cliched fight to follow, but that initial burst of violence certainly comes as a jolt and is excitingly choreographed.<br />VERDICT: Totally unpredictable and therefore one to cherish.<br /><br />.........................<br /><br />So, if you love STAR TREK and can ignore the occasional nagging voice of the inner purist, this is an essential purchase.<br /><br />VERY HIGHLY RECOMMENDEDs Kirk exits the turbolift on the 'mirror' Enterprise, the flat hand (not a fist, a hand) of an assassin thumps him on the face dead centre. Secondly the book is highly readable, and like other Archer books, one that is hard to put down for mundane stuff like eating and sleeping! However, it does have some relatively big faults! I was somewhat disappointed that 2 of the main characters in the book (I'll not tell you who they are so as not to be a spoiler; you can work it out for yourself) are IMO \\"short changed\\" in that one of them literally disappears for 4 years, out of a 6 1/2 year time span for the book, and the other for three, with only a brief explanation over their \\"missing\\" years before they appear again, which I found to be very annoying. It seemed to me this was also to keep the book to a \\"short\\" 339 pages which is also an annoyance, when you consider the 500-700 page length of most of Mr. Archer's other books! I also dislike this trend among authors of taking a good sized novel and adding a middle book as a \\"filler\\" to create a trilogy to extend/increase their revenues and Mr. Archer does that here, and as I said before, this novel is just a bridge to a final one! I find this crassly exploitive of reader's loyalties! At least Mr. Archer, so far, writes all the books himself, unlike other authors, such as James Patterson, who puts his name to some of his co-authored books that I believe are largely written by other authors, who quite frankly would have a hard time getting their ideas published, another pet peeve of mine.<br /><br />So overall a decent \\"bridging\\" book and read but an expensive one (for its length/plot), but hopefully the final one will be much, much better. I think 3 stars is a very fair rating for this book, at best!

0345803485|5.0|After reading the first book of the trilogy, I had many mixed feelings about the book and a curiosity about how others liked or disliked it.  So here I am, a clinical psychologist by training and in life, reading Amazon reviews.  About 2/3 of the reviews were either 1 or 5 stars, which probably mirrors what the novel elicits in others.  If one is looking for a good S&M novel to stimulate them, I'd pass on it.  Though probably erotic to some, I highly doubt that the sexual aspects of the book mirror any reality (other than one's first love and sexual discovery). More important, it (the eroticism) appears to be a secondary theme that is an extreme metaphor for the very true difficulties in what love means and how people love.<br /><br />\\"Fifty Shades of Grey\\" (Book 1) was captivating, not for its sexual themes, but for its focus on the need for personal control and power, and how (yet unknown) early childhood trauma can shape one's sexual experiences for life.  It is ironic that Christian's idealized childhood (after his adoption at age 4) yielded such a disturbed sexuality whereas Ana's somewhat chaotic childhood (child of multiple divorces) led to such disparate sexual needs as adults.  \\"Fifty Shades of Grey\\" is also about love and what one may need to give up or accept for that love.  It portrays a push/pull relationship on both parts.<br /><br />Finally, \\"Fifty Shades of Grey\\" helps inform that all relationships are shades of gray rather than good or bad.  Explaining shades of gray in the book, submissives can have the ultimate power (viz., dominance) by their ability to say \\"stop\\" and dominants may have an unspoken need to have that control stripped from them or at least the possibility of it. There is no true 100% dominant nor a 100% submissive.<br /><br />Many people incorporate aspects of dominant and submissive behaviors into their sexual play but my understanding (limited, I'll admit) of true S&M needs is that sexual feelings can only be elicited in that specific context ... more akin to fetishes or paraphilia than sexual play.<br /><br />I already purchased the second novel of the Trilogy (thinking it was the first and then had to download the first book onto my iPad to read in the correct order) and am anxious to read if and how Christian's ambivalence about letting himself be loved is resolved and if Anastasia can hang in there for the distance.

0374275637|5.0|This book sums up a lifetime of experimentation in the areas of judgment and decisionmaking.  Kaheman earned a Nobel laureate for this work.  He's an excellent writer too, and the 38 chapters -- each 10 pages long -- are nicely organized and tightly presented.  Anyone will be able to understand his work, and I suspect most everyone will be shaken up.<br /><br />Here is the main point. In ways we can understand and predict, we make systematic errors in judgment throughout the day. It's probably because of natural selection we're good at making quick judgments, and although they are often good enough they are also rife with shortcuts, exaggerations and mistakes, and these are capped off neatly with overconfidence.<br /><br />He says the mind has two operating systems, which he labels 1 and 2. The first is the intuitive mind, the transparent bit that recognizes a pencil as a pencil, or a face as angry or sad, and chooses between fight and flight. It also judges the size of numbers, keeps track of time, recalls memories, and basically takes the first quick swing at everything. We consider this \\"knowing.\\" System 2, what you might call \\"thinking\\" it includes estimation, calculation, and all manner of figuring out - much of which happens very fast and in general we're at least partially aware of it. Both systems cut corners, jump to conclusions, omit inconvenient information, exaggerate and guess. System 2's biggest problem, according to Kahneman, is laziness. It leaves System 1 in charge, and when called on it does the minimum required. Generally this means piecing together a credible solution, then quitting.<br /><br />This dichotomy seemed at first a bit forced to me, as I thought there must be a continuum instead, but it turned out to be a fabulous way to parse out how, when, and why our thinking can go wrong.<br /><br />Things we encounter first hand seem more important and more common than they should, as do things we can easily recall. When the chance of something (good or bad) is small we tend to either ignore or exaggerate the possibility of it happening. When judging an experience retroactively we focus on the peaks (highs and lows) and the end - duration doesn't count for much. We consider losses about twice as important as an equal gain. Thoughts immediately preceding can dramatically influence a judgment. In retrospect, assessing an event, we give far too little weight to blind luck. Considering options separately often results in different assessments than joint consideration. We use stereotypes to draw inferences from the group to the individual, and the other way around. We're bad at statistics. Consider this:<br /><br />A woman is seen reading the New York Times on the New York subway. Which of the following is a better bet about her level of education?<br />1. She has a PhD<br />2. She does not have a college degree<br /><br />If you chose the first, as I did, think again. There are a lot more nongraduates than PhDs riding the subway. We overlooked the base data.<br /><br />Here's a clever one:<br />Hearing that the highest incidences of kidney cancer in U.S. counties are found in the rural, sparsely populated states you might guess the reason: these places are poorer, less educated, with less access to health care, where people have high-fat diets, drink and smoke. That would certainly make some sense. It's a coherent, compelling and completely fabricated story.<br />If you had heard instead that these same areas have the lowest rates of kidney cancer, you might come up with this one: rural living is healthier, with fresh air, fresh locally grown food, more exercise and less stress than in urban areas. In either case we are able not only to throw up an explanation, we also diminish all effect of the countervailing arguments. We want a satisfactory explanation so our lazy System 2 can go back to rest. That's my story, in other words, and I'm sticking to it.<br /><br />So which of these two statements about kidney cancer is true? Both are true; you see, we also are bad with statistics. There are fewer people in rural counties, the measure is cases/1,000 people, so in rural counties the denominator is small. Random events in the numerator will cause large changes in the rate. Example: If a disease affects 20% of the greater population, in counties with just 2 people the rate is likely to be 0%. It could be 50% or (rarely) 100%. But it will never reflect the actual average. This is a statistical phenomenon that even statisticians overlook.<br /><br />Some of the findings in the book as so bizarre I still have my doubts, although the research seems sound, has been replicated, the results are consistent, and the effects are strong. For example, when subjects were asked to estimate the percentage of African nations which are members of the UN it mattered whether the respondents had just spun a 10 or 65 on a (rigged) \\"random\\" wheel. Their estimates for UN membership averaged 25% vs 45%, respectively! While people are holding a sequence of numbers in their head they have less self-control eating cake! Or if they grip a pencil between their teeth (forcing a smile) cartoons seem funnier to them, than when they hold it between pursed lips (forcing a frown). People pencil-frowning will concentrate more strongly on the task at hand. And people required to nod were more likely to agree with an editorial they listen to than those who were told to shake their heads. These results just seems absurd. Yet, I must admit: me calling them absurd is no evidence at all. It's just incredulity, which I've decided to set aside, as best I can.<br /><br />On the contrary, I found myself falling for many of the little test-yourself demonstrations in the book. Consider this question:<br />Was Ghandi 130 years old when he died? You'll know he wasn't. But how old was he, when he died? The typical response is significantly a larger number, compared to subjects who were first asked if he had died at the age of40. The first group would tend to adjust from 130 downward until they hit ages they thought were plausible; and the others do the same upward from 40. Someone who figured Ghandi was between 65 and 85 might guess nearly a 20 year difference, depending only on the anchor they had been primed with.<br /><br />Anchors are one of many points that can slip in to influence our thinking. Here are some others:<br /><br />The availability heuristic (129), confidence by coherence(209), the illusion of validity (211), the illusion of skill (212), the illusion of intuition/ strength of algorithms (223) , the hindsight bias (202), theillusion of control (259), the halo effect (199), nonregression to the mean (175), inside vs. outside view (248), the planning fallacy (250), stereotyping (168) , representativeness (149), the substitution effect(139), probability neglect (144), statistical and causal base rates (168), the conjunction fallacy (158), theory induced blindness (277), risk aversion (270), loss aversion (284), the endowment effect (293),bad news bias (301), possibility effect (311), certainty effect (311), the expectation principle (312), denominator neglect (329), and overweighting (333). There is narrow vs. broad framing (336), reference points (281), the sunk cost fallacy (345), and the evaluability hypothesis(360), and the effect of categories (356). The focusing illusion (402) is a simple one, with reach: (\\"nothing in life is as important as you think it is when you are thinking about it\\"), as is WYSIATI (153: \\"what you see is all there is\\"). And Kahneman's prospect theory (circa 1979: with lifelong collaborator Amos Tversky) is developed throughout the book. They showed that people evaluate risks on the basis of losses and gains from a reference point, and not simply on outcomes as utility theory would suggest.<br /><br />This all may seem like a chore to work through, but it's not. The writing is very clear and engaging, and the 38 well-crafted chapters are each, remarkably, 10 pages long. The book has plenty of examples, end notes with references, concluding advice, a couple full reprints in two appendices, and lots of little demonstrations you can try on yourself. Another example of these. Which of these two opportunities would you prefer?:<br /><br />1. a gamble that offers a 10% chance to win $95 and 90% chance to lose $5?<br />2. or would you pay $5 for a 10% chance to win $100 and a 90% chance to win nothing?<br /><br />Most people find the second one more attractive, but look again. The fact that they are identical is difficult to see because we are more adverse to losing $5 than we are to paying $5. Here's another: You are considering surgery (with slightly better long-term results than radiation therapy) and you were told, about the surgery<br /><br />1. The one-month survival rate is 90%<br />2. There is a 10% mortality in the first month.<br /><br />Doesn't the first one sound better? Or this:<br /><br />* Adam switches from a gas-guzzler of 12 mpg to a slightly less voracious guzzler: 14 mpg<br />* Beth switches from a 30 mpg car to one that runs at 40 mpg<br /><br />Who will save more gas by switching? The answer is Adam, by a large margin.<br /><br />One of the best sections of the book is the last. It describes two very distinct senses of self: one is the experiencing self and the other, the remembering self. When someone asks \\"how are you?\\" you might hear \\"how's your mood right now?\\" but are more likely to respond to \\"how have things been going?\\" ... in other words ... \\"how do you like your memories?\\" It's the remembering self that most people care about, whether they're looking at their own lives (it's why we take pictures on vacations) or others'. When subjects were asked to imagine taking a dream vacation, but afterward all memory of it would be erased, most would not send themselves (or another amnesic) to climb over mountains or go trekking through the jungle. \\"These experiences are mostly painful in realtime and gain value from the expectation that both the pain and the joy of reaching the goal will be memorable.\\" \\"Odd as it may seem,\\" Kahneman concludes, \\"I am my remembering self, and the experiencing self, who does my living, is like a stranger to me.\\"<br /><br />Unfortunately, it's a little worse still, because the remembering self doesn't represent the experiencing self very well. Looking back, durations of experiences don't matter much. Just two things do: 1. peaks (good or bad), and 2. how it ended. Kahneman explains how education, religion, illness, poverty, wealth, and other factors impact the two forms of satisfaction.<br /><br />It's a great book, I believe it's a full life's work, and my criticisms are minor. I thought that maybe not enough distinction was made between one-time judgments and iterated ones. In the Prisoner's Dilemma, for example, once-off games lead to mutual defection but when done repeatedly with the same players (as in the real world) it leads instead to altruism. Some of the results in a psychology lab may be quite different in the real world. There are also a few surprising typos, like a mix-up on page 335, where \\"AD\\" and \\"BC\\" are switched, throwing off the explanation; and on page 337 two miscalculations in the formulas actually invalidate the results.<br /><br />But if these are the low points, they are not very low. The peaks were many and very high (for me) and even though I felt the last chapter might have been slightly overreaching, it ended well oo. It took a while to read, but duration doesn't count for much -- I'm very pleased. It will sit on my top shelf, next to Pinker.<br /><br />(Ghandi died at 79, BTW)

The premise of the novel is a game organised by a Government built on the remnants of what used to be the USA, featuring a group of children of all ages brought together in a large fenced area, and asked to fight to death with each other until there is only one last, successful fighter. The game is filmed: reality TV at its worst. I have a problem with premises featuring children killing each other for pleasure (not that those in the book really want to kill each other to start with); you just don't want those ideas in our children's minds. But then again, this is fiction. I loved \\"Lord of the Flies\\" by William Golding, a terrible story about children stuck on a remote island and starting to kill each other, but the premise was different (a reenactment of society, not a game) and it was really an adult book at the end of the day. Having said that, I really liked the Hunger Games. It think it is innovative, clever and has a great plot. I'm looking forward to reading the sequel.

How did they go about selecting producers to work with? Not covered. Also, the movie would have benefited from a consistent voice-over narration to tie all of the pieces together. There was some narration, but not enough. The musical-chair drummer situation was covered in under 30-seconds, over an hour into the movie; but this was a huge issue and influenced the band's sound as they moved from one drummer to the next. These differing *sounds* were not explored. The band was never asked to \\"give me your thoughts\\" on each and every album (great, good, bad, bomb), or \\"which album was your favorite?\\", or \\"which songs are your favorite?\\", or \\"were you consciously trying to change your sound, or did that just happen/evolve?\\". And, how about more info on their song writing process. We learned that Eddie took over from Stone somewhere around Vitology, but that was it. The deepest nugget was the seeming admission that the band recoiled (perhaps subconsciously) from success. These guys never really wanted to be this famous -- this should have been explored more. I shouldn't be able to learn more from Wikipedia than I did from watching this documentary. After the first hour it became apparent that the film just was not going to dig deeper. Don't get me wrong, it certainly has some entertainment and nostalgic value, but it falls far short of what it could have been. So, what could it have been, you ask? See the Foo Fighters \\"Back and Forth\\".

B00723NWJE|3.0|I must be in the minority for thinking Jones is an overrated artist. I mean, right from her debut she was a darling. She came along and gave popular music some soft jazz touches back in 2002 and ended up selling 10 million plus. But I have stuck with her for some reason. I have bought each of her albums and found the same problem each time--a handful of gems padded by plodding background muzak. It's not as if Jones demonstrates her musical virtuosity or lyrical prowess. No, she strikes me as average on both fronts.<br /><br />But there is something about her music. There was \\"Come Away With Me\\" and \\"I Don't Know Why\\", or \\"Sunrise\\" and \\"Creepin' In\\", or \\"Wish I Could\\" and \\"Broken\\" or \\"Chasing Pirates\\" and \\"Young Blood\\" or \\"Bull Rider\\" and \\"Take Off Your Cool\\". Each of these songs showcase the best in Norah Jones and her ability to actually vary her sound. Unfortunately, this has not translated to an entire album of gems.<br /><br />\\"Little Broken Hearts\\" is no different, and I found myself thinking the same thing again--when I listen to the album all the way through it is rather monotonous. This album was being hailed and promoted as a dark and drastic style change but it isn't. It's Norah Jones, just less jazzy. But this started with her last album, 2009's \\"The Fall\\". She doesn't just attack the other woman, but she also toys with her and even sympathizes with her as well.<br /><br />So, three and a half stars. Jones has much room to mature and grow as an artist. I bought the deluxe version from Target that had the three bonus tracks, and \\"I Don't Wanna Hear Another Sound\\" is the best of the three. Overall, \\"Little Broken Hearts\\" isn't the best break-up album I've ever heard, and it's not the most interesting meditation on love and relationships either. But, it manages to hang together relatively well even if it comes off as monotonous at times in terms of its musical approach.<br /><br />Here's how \\"Little Broken Hearts\\" compares to Norah Jones' other works:<br /><br />2002 Come Away With Me: Three and a Half Stars<br />2004 Feels Like Home: Three Stars<br />2007 Not Too Late: Two Stars<br />2009 The Fall: Four Stars<br />2010 ...Featuring Norah Jones: Three Stars<br />2012 Little Broken Hearts: Three and a Half Starsm to mature and grow as an artist. 0374203032|4.0|My first introduction to Michael Sandel was the iTunes lectures on political philosophy lectures he gave at Harvard. I watched each of his lectures twice, and soaked in Professor Sandel's insights and superb teaching style. The lectures prompted me to read numerous philosophical works of the past, as well as some of Sandel's other works. I knew I had to read his newest book when I saw it in the philosophy section of the book store the other day, and I feel a better person for having done so.<br /><br />The author states that he does not have the answers to many of the questions and ideas raised in his book but that his overall goal is to get a discussion started. A discussion about whether or not our society should allow the free markets to enter any and all aspects of our lives. Sandel aptly argues that the free market is not a system that is blind to morals and ethics. He relies on two ideas that are brought up time and time again throughout the book: 1) inequality and 2) corruption. One of the author's points that has been a bit of a game changer for me is the idea that markets do not necessarily get goods into the hands of those who value them most. He uses an example of great seats at a baseball game to demonstrate his point. Sandel says that those who are willing to pay the most for great baseball tickets may not be those who value them most highly but merely those who have the deepest pockets. Surely there are kids watching games on TV who know every stat, collect every card, and worship players beyond individuals with the deepest pockets. For many, purchasing a ticket represents a large percentage of their income, which in turn represents their higher valuing of the ticket than the individual who is spending a much smaller percentage of his/her income to purchase a great ticket. This is such a simple principle but one which I had not given much thought to previously. This idea of markets not always getting goods into the hands of those who value them most highly is found throughout the book.<br /><br />Sandel uses so many recent examples to illustrate his many points, that this book is not only a great introduction into ethics and values but also a great primer into modern situations/events that affect each of us. I found the examples to be very helpful. I also found myself disagreeing with many of his suppositions. I admit that I am a bit of a Nietzschean, and Sandel's idea's of right and wrong are sometimes frustrating. For people like myself who believe strongly in moral relativism and social constructionism, it was difficult to accept Sandel's views on things being good or things being inherently bad. Of course as an adherent of many of Nietzsche's ideas I also realized that we are therefore free to defend whatever ethical principles we choose, provided they has a basis in logic. Sandel, for the most part is quite logical, and rather calm in the face of such partisan issues.<br /><br />Sandel's book is rather short but incredibly concise. I was amazed at how much he was able to cover in 203 pages. I found myself questioning many of my beliefs about how the world should work, and that is always a wonderful feeling. I would highly recommend this book to anyone interested in bettering society, politics, economics, or philosophy. The author is to be commended for succeeding in what was for me, the beginning of a discussion of ideas I was previously sure I had the answers to. The binding is good, the paper quality more than acceptable, and the type set attractive.ghly is found throughout the book.<br /><br />Sandel uses so many recent examples to illustrate his many points, that this book is not only a great introduction into ethics and values but also a great primer into modern situations/events that affect each of us. I found the examples to be very helpful. I also found myself disagreeing with many of his suppositions. I admit that I am a bit of a Nietzschean, and Sandel's idea's of right and wrong are sometimes frustrating. By doing so, the authors render a clear service in collecting the information presented in the book and furthering our understanding of the ubiquity, frequency and depth of financial turmoil. Regrettably, their research shows that our current financial circumstances are more difficult than usual.<br /><br />I had hoped to derive useful insights for investing. I have read other books - The Big Short, Dying of Money, Lords of Finance, etc. - with this same purpose. However, while I was greatly impressed with the authors' effort and commitment, the main lesson that I drew from the book is that financial distress is quite common and recovery from it can be painful and lengthy. I expect our recovery to be so.<br /><br />The book may aid an investor if one invests overseas. The historical tendencies of individual countries may be of some use.  An investor may avoid some countries because of their financial history. For instance, if one loans money to Argentina or Greece with an expectation of repayment, you are quite likely to get what you deserve.<br /><br />The book notes that inflation is one common political response to national financial distress. Indeed, I have read a blog by Professor Rogoff observing that the United States' current overcommitted financial circumstances might be eased by inflation. This, too, probably lies in our future. The book, Dying of Money, about the German hyperinflation of the 1920's may provide some practical guidance on this point.

0765310015|2.0|This review is about the entire series rather than simply the first book (GotM), as I can't seem to find a page on Amazon regarding the whole series. In any case, what you get in GotM is what you get in the rest of the books, so it is applicable.<br /><br />I have enjoyed fantasy epics for a long time, since reading fluff like the Belgariad when I was a child. I'm also one of those people that, once a book is begun, cannot put it down until finishing, no matter how awful - and since I purchased the entire Malazan series...<br /><br />Much of what I'm about to write has already been mentioned scores of times in the negative or somewhat negative reviews this book has received. I'll divide it into the good, the bad, and the ugly.<br /><br />The Good (or things I liked): As many have mentioned the world-building highlights Erikson's imagination, there are a wide variety of protagonists in the book, which go beyond what you typically find in a fantasy epic (mage, swordsman, thief etc), and this adds some originality. Some of the devices used to highlight a character's personality are original and entertaining - Iskaral Pust, Greyfrog, Kruppe. There are also moments and passages in this series which are well worth the read - some that come to mind are the battle of Pale (GotM), the introduction of Karsa Orlong (HoC), the early part of MT regarding the Tiste Edur...they are the moments when the plot is focused, when the characters have important roles to play and you stay with them long enough to see development - or when the action is fast paced and well described, and leaves you wanting to find out what happens. Unfortunately these moments are simply too few and far between. Some of the character and place names are fairly cool.<br /><br />The Bad (or things I disliked): Place names and character names are, many times, silly beyond belief, as if Erikson ran out of ideas and recruited his 6 year old nephew to help him out. He spends long, sometimes unbearable amounts of time, philosophizing through his characters. I can't count the amount of times I found myself wanting to skip entire pages, knowing they'd bore me to tears. The books could have been reduced in length (and improved in quality) had there been more focus, or as someone suggested, a better editor. There are simply too many characters - I enjoy complex, intricate works, but too much of anything is never a good thing, and here Erikson goes overboard, filling each book with character after character, to the developmental detriment of all of them.<br /><br />The Ugly (or things that make it bad): Until now what I mentioned were subjective observations, things I personally liked or disliked but which may inspire different feelings in other readers. Maybe some people enjoy huge swathes of philosophy being spouted by characters you suspect never received an education (but who cares about consistency?), maybe you enjoy infinite characters or maybe you dislike focused storytelling; however, trying to be more objective, Erikson fails on many levels which are prerequisites for good writing. Characters are introduced with no rhyme or reason, no real effect on anything. Then they, or others who seem to be built up as important, disappear for no good reason. Plots and subplots fizzle out for no apparent reason, or are left hanging. Many things simply happen for, like I said, apparently no good reason, other than to fill out the books. Deus Ex Machina is the order of the day, present in inverse proportion to consistency. And at times the writing itself is god-awful, messy, convoluted stuff, painful to read.<br /><br />I get the feeling that Erikson wanted to participate in what has been, for a while, a trend to create vast fantasy epics, and that he has sacrificed quality and focus for the sake of scope, to the ultimate detriment of the series itself, and his readers. Some people will love it, but some people also loved Baywatch. Next to ASOFAI (yes, very different in many ways, but filled with good writing, coherent character development and plots) this just doesn't come close to standing up.

B0079J27SS|5.0|I've been a fan of these recordings for over 40 years and they sound better than ever in these new EMI DSD/SACD remasterings.  But folks shouldn't expect miracles.  Not even DSD/SACD technology can make a fifty year old analog recording sound as if it had been recorded yesterday.  That said, I thank EMI for taking the trouble to apply the newest technology to older recordings like these because the benefit is real, even though that benefit registers as a greater subtlety in the projection of texture, not in a super Hi-Fi transformation of the original recordings.  With Klemperer performances, greater textural subtlety yields large rewards because he was a master at balancing orchestral choirs, at making everything in a classical composition \\"sound\\" clearly and in perspective.  In short, the new remastering helps us better hear what Klemperer was trying to achieve.  The fact remains that the newest recordings in this set sound best overall.<br /><br />As for the performances, they are what they are.  HIP fanatics probably will dismiss them as archaic and irrelevant, while the \\"speed and drama\\" crowd will be disappointed that they aren't more obviously emotive and colorful.  I am no uncritical Klemperer admirer, and I enjoy HIP-informed performances and any other interesting take on the classic masters.  But Klemperer remains a conductor I turn to again and again for stimulation and inspiration. His great accomplishment with Haydn, Mozart, and Beethoven in particular was to produce un-Romantic performances that sound inevitable and \\"right\\"; sober and straightforward, yes, but also intellectually compelling and emotionally satisfying.  If you also admire Klemperer's way with the classics, the new DSD/SACD remastering makes his accomplishment sound with greater clarity and presence and I think you too will be happy to have these CDs.<br /><br />A couple of final notes.  These are stereo recordings that, in most cases, represent Klemperer's final studio productions of this music for EMI.  (Earlier EMI mono recordings of some of these symphonies have been reissued by the Testament label.)  As 'Unclehulot' points out in his comment below my review, this set includes Klemperer's earlier (1956) stereo recording of the G minor symphony (No. 40),  rather than the later (1960/1961) performance that EMI identifies in its liner note listing.  In the earlier recording, Klemperer's tempi are faster (even fierce) and the performance is more darkly dramatic. Unclehulot also points out that the earlier recording includes the Exposition repeat, omitted in the later performance. The downside of using that earlier recording in previous CD issues was the presence of a large amount of background (tape) hiss very characteristic of mastertape recordings in the early 1950s, and which previous CD remasterings removed only by compromising sonic presence. The new remastering provides a very satisfactory improvement for a classic recording that is now pushing 60 years old!  Final, final: Klemperer was a Manic Depressive and when he recorded during his manic phases his performances sometimes achieved a unique emotional incandescence, but at other times they just exhibit unpersuasive speed and violence. On the evidence presented here, I'd bet money that Klemperer was in one of his manic phases during the sessions that produced the \\"Linz\\" Symphony (No. 36).  It's a fine performance with quick tempi, but it just doesn't achieve the miraculous sense of rightness-in-proportion that the other performances in this set demonstrate again and again. IMHO.

Sy - a gifted physical and verbal comedian - had our audience convulsed, at times, with laughter. He simply treats Philippe as he would anyone else. B003JSSPSY|1.0|I liked Season 1, but Season 2 bugged me.<br /><br />The biggest reason was that, if you get to the end of it, loose ends are left unresolved (probably with the idea that they would keep a bad guy in the picture for Season 3) and there's no plans for Season 3.  So, my biggest warning is this: IF YOU WATCH SEASON 2 BE FOREWARNED THAT YOU WILL NEVER, EVER FIND OUT IF THE BAD GUYS ARE FINALLY AND ULTIMATELY DEFEATED BECAUSE THERE'S NO NEXT SEASON.<br /><br />My second issue is, if you're like me, you want plots to make logical sense.  Having too many plot flaws messes up my enjoyment of a story so much that all I think about are the plot flaws and not the story.<br /><br />Here are the plot flaws (omitting plot spoilers):<br />-1-<br />The biggest plot flaw of all is the entire premise of Season 2: that the Keeper of the Underworld is trying to destroy all life.  Think about it... if you raise cattle for meat and you kill all of them, no more pregnancies = no more calves = no more cattle = no more meat.<br /><br />If the goal of the Keeper is to get as many souls as possible and he had any intelligence, he'd want the status quo: that generations grow, live and die and that the world is full of disease, war and natural disasters (which it certainly already is).  So, if the Keeper's goal is to kill all life, thereby stopping the cycle of generations and thereby stop having generation after generation go to him, he's an idiot because he'll end up with less souls long term!<br /><br />-2-<br />Fortunately, the stupidity of the Keeper is matched by the stupidity of the good guys.  Shortly into the season, Richard is unaccountably having headaches.  Three middle-aged women who are complete strangers appear to him and say that this is because he has magical powers and that he'll die of his headaches if they don't take him off to another world and train him to use his latent magic powers.<br /><br />So, what does he do?  Does he do something sensible like wait until he can talk to Zedd about all this (I mean, surely Zedd can train him to use his magic without him having to go off to another world and without him having to trust people he's never met)?  No!  He just trusts the three women and runs off with them to another world, leaving Zedd, Kaylan and his quest behind.<br /><br />PLOT SPOILER: I could forgive this if it wasn't for the fact that it turns out that he CAN'T trust them.  In fact, he ends up giving all of his magic to a bad guy in order to escape from them.  If he hadn't trusted them in the first place and just consulted Zedd, none of that would have happened.<br /><br />Yet, all of this and the consequences figure prominently in the rest of the season.  There's no sense of Richard being foolish and learning from his mistakes.  He's portrayed as just a straight up good guy who did what he \\"had\\" to do, when he didn't have to.<br /><br />-3-<br />Another piece of stupidity is from Zedd the wizard.  The good guys are often attacked by bad girls called Mord Sith, who absorb magic.  So, Zedd simply never attacks them and sometimes even ends up getting successfully threatened by them into doing something he doesn't want to do.<br /><br />Hasn't he ever heard of lateral thinking?  Who says he has to cast a spell on them?  Surely a Wizard of the First Order (which he is) could, say, make the grass grab their feet or make the ground swallow them or something (neither of which would be direct attacks).  Or... maybe he could summon spirits to attack them (also not a direct attack).  I'm just brainstorming, but I'd think he could come up with better ideas yet.<br /><br />Even if he had no way of dealing with them, we know that he often turns invisible.  Yet, when they capture him or manage to put an agiel (their torture weapons) to his throat, he doesn't bother.  Why doesn't he just turn invisible, run away and then return to help his friends when the opportunity presents itself (or wreak havoc with the Mord Sith while invisible, like tripping them with a stick or something)?<br /><br />-4-<br />Okay, now I'm encroaching on plot spoilers, so I can only say so much.  There's a spell that Zedd casts in the second to last episode, which he has to undo.  The problem is that undoing it makes no sense, if you stop and think about it.  I'll just say that if you do X and then undo Y, it doesn't undo X, yet somehow the vicarious logic of the plot tries to get us to believe that by undoing Y, Zedd can undo X, even though it doesn't follow logically at all.<br /><br />-5-<br />In the last episode, of course, the Keeper is the ultimate enemy who has to be reckoned with.  The thing is that the Keeper has a gigantic opportunity to win by doing something really obvious (again, I won't spoil the plot), and yet he stupidly does something else that he hopes will assure him victory, but which isn't nearly as likely to succeed as the other thing (hint for those who have seen Season 2: if your enemy can succeed if you go in the vicinity of X, it's typically a good idea to go in the opposite direction, not just to vicariously decide to go to X for no reason other than that the writers need you to).<br /><br />But, then, we already knew that the Keeper was stupid (see plot flaw 1).<br /><br />Season 1 ended satisfactorily with Richard defeating Darken Rahl.  It should have stayed that way.  My advice is watch Season 1 and not Season 2.  That way, you'll end the nice, fairly logical plotting of Season 1 with the epic hero victorious instead of having to slog through 22 episodes of nonsense just to have loose-ends not tied up and never find out what happens.

0307352145|5.0|*A full summary of this book is available here: [[ASIN:B00HMD4VF8 An Executive Summary of Susan Cain's 'Quiet: The Power of Introverts in a World That Can't Stop Talking']]<br /><br />Being the quieter, more reserved type, introverts are not as inclined as others to broadcast just who they are and what makes them tick, much less honk their own horns. However, given that Western culture has increasingly pushed introverts aside, and is intent on celebrating their opposite, it is high time that introverts stepped out of character, made themselves heard, and proclaimed to the world that they have much to offer indeed. This is the campaign that Susan Cain launches in her new book, `Quiet: The Power of Introverts in a World That Can't Stop Talking'.<br /><br />Cain begins her account by way of establishing that Western culture has increasingly adopted an `Extrovert Ideal,' in which louder, bolder, more ebullient and risk-friendly individuals are valued over and above the quieter, more reserved, reflective and heed-friendly ones. While Western culture has a long history of favoring the extrovert, Cain argues that this bias has steepened since the industrial revolution, and particularly in the past century as the West has become ever-more urbanized and commercial. Over the course of this time-frame, Cain argues, a Culture of Personality, perhaps best represented by the motivational guru Tony Robins, has come to replace a Culture of Character, best represented by such figures as Abraham Lincoln.<br /><br />Cain's intention here is not to put-down extroverts, or to say that they are inferior to introverts. Rather, her argument is that the latter have an important role to play in many areas of society that is now often overlooked. For one, the introvert's greater willingness to listen to others and their input makes them better leaders than is generally recognized. Second, their heed-friendly temperaments serves to better protect them (and those around them) against dangerous situations, and makes them particularly valuable in such professions as financial investing, where undue risk is not only known to get individuals in trouble, but entire nations, and even the entire international community. Third, the fact that introverts tend to have a sharpened moral sense makes them well-suited to fill the role of the social conscience of society, which is often valuable in protecting the downtrodden, and also in saving societies from their own recklessness.<br /><br />Finally, the added thoughtfulness and persistence of introverts, and their heightened capacity to work independently, often gives them an edge in creative enterprises such as art and technological innovation, as well as in more intellectual industries such as science and engineering. Indeed, Cain insists that there is plenty of evidence to indicate that working independently is an important part of having and developing the best ideas, not only for introverts but for everyone. This helps explain why the most creative people tend to be introverted, and also serves as an argument in favor of tempering the emphasis on groupthink and collaborative work that is currently running rampant through our schools and businesses.<br /><br />While introverts often have more to offer than many recognize, it is also the case that their sensitive nature tends to make them more fragile than others; as such, they are particularly susceptible to having their talents stifled and even snuffed out before they have had the time to develop. For this reason, Cain argues, it is especially important for parents and educators to know the best approaches when it comes to both raising and educating the quieter type, and the author makes a concerted effort to address these issues here. In particular, Cain emphasizes just how vital it is to encourage and nurture the introvert's peculiar talents, and to be patient in dealing with their inwardness. Having said this, Cain does not advocate giving in to this inwardness entirely, as she stresses the importance of challenging the introvert to come out of their shell as much as they are able, in order that they may learn to make their voices heard, and to get along in a more extrovert-friendly world. In connection with this, Cain argues that it is not only possible, but often healthy and beneficial for introverts to stretch themselves to be more extroverted on occasion--especially when it is in the name of a goal that they value, and as long as it is not overdone.<br /><br />Altogether, the work is well researched and very insightful, and there is much to be learned here about the quieter among us for the extroverted and introverted alike--and also much to help the latter feel more comfortable in their own skin (thankfully without being an exercise in self-congratulation for them). The results were phenomenal and the album by this reviewer was described as his most defining moment to date. Montreal is a live recording that in some ways was 10 years in the making considering that it encompasses his entire recording catalog that began back in 1995. And without a doubt, Montreal equals and probably exceeds the achievement of his prior effort making this the new standard not only for Cook but arguably anyone who wishes to document his or her achievements live.<br /><br />Opening with the atmospheric \\"Beloved\\" with eastern chants, surging drums and percussion, the audience is already in a frenzy as Cook arrives on the stage. Jesse wastes no time in making full use of his axe, plucking his strings at an exceptional speed. The beauty of Cook is that while he is technically brilliant he never replaces his pyrotechnic workouts at the expense of a gorgeous melody. Jump forward and checkout the bouncy but almost folksy feel of \\"Breeze From Saintes Maries\\". It is sequenced back to back with the energetic calypso pulsation of \\"Jumpstart\\", which includes the percussion repartee of Paul Antonio and Art Avalos. B000002JNJ|5.0|Cowboys From Hell was the groundbreaking, and now legendary, re-invention of Pantera, who had previously been a make-up, lipstick, and hairspray glam metal band.  Having gone virtually nowhere with that angle, Phil Anselmo, Diamond Darrell (later to be known as Dimebag), Rex (formerly known as Rex Rocker), and Vinnie Paul took the band in a heavier direction, laying the foundation for their highly influential groove metal style that basically ended the thrash era of American metal.  The songs on CFH still retain some touches of their earlier sound, such as Phil's occasional high-pitched vibrato, but mostly show the great diversity Pantera would continue to incorporate and build upon.  The title track, Psycho Holiday, and Medicine Man are fine examples of the bouncy, mid-tempo groove that spawned the entire metalcore subgenre.  Primal Concrete Sledge and Message In Blood show slower, grinding and churning grooves that are heavy, but not quite thrash.  Domination and The Art of Shredding tend more toward thrash, with faster riffing and drums.  Nothing on this album really sounds quite like the crunch of the next few releases that would define their identity and go on to influence every band in metal, from the underground thrash bands to the heavyweights of the genre, like Metallica.  CFH is more of a bridge between their past and what they were trying to become.  But it has tons of groove, killer riffs and solos, and a shift into more aggressive music and vocals.  Anyone who is a fan of metal and doesn't already have this should correct that mistake and pick it up.  Pantera was one of the most important bands ever to emerge in the history of heavy music.

B000002GXS|5.0|From the back alleys and whiskey bars of urban America, The Heart of Saturday Night proclaimed Tom Waits a new talent on a level very few had expected, but many would praise. Waits had done his time in rundown hotels and one night stands, been through his debut album, Closing Time, and was finally street seasoned enough to get it right. All the hallmarks of his style were here, songs that ached of love and loss, sparse jazz arrangements, and his growling beatnik vocal phrasings. Together these traits created a cult following that spoke reverently about his work, and shared his albums between friends like secret rituals of the rite of passage. Waits knew what it was like to be conspicuously miserable, and could portray it in his songs like liquid moonlight dripping out of the sky. New Coat of Paint opens things up with a regressing back beat that spits you out into the street corner looking to score a slight bit of affection with a girl bar hoping at your side, \\"You wear a dress baby, I'll wear a tie, we'll laugh at that old blood shot moon in that burgundy sky.\\" Then San Diego Serenade predisposes you into a relationship that's better the further the view gets in the rear view mirror and begs the listener to question why they didn't look back until it was too late. Semi Suite takes the listener inside a truck driver's world as he views his life through pain soaked excuses of everyday wear and tear relationships. It's filled to the brim with the point of view of his lady friend wanting to pull away but always there when he returns. The relationship battles Waits writes about he must of seen a thousand times in late night rundown joints where the stories flowed fasted than the alcohol and he paid attention and drank them down with a memory only he could digest. When Shiver Me Timbers sets sail we see that Waits can write about relationships from the perspective of many working class men, truckers, men of the sea, busboys, and even bums. He met every one of them and endeared his self to them for these stories, he knew his people better than any politician could ever dream of. The highlights of the album is Waits' zigzagging bass back beat driven hitchhikers jazz poem, Diamonds on my Windshield. The song oozes beatnik cool as Waits finger snaps his way through the song as if he's catching the next ride himself. Don't worry you'll be on the ride too. (Looking For) The Heart of Saturday Night is a timepiece drama that reflects the Saturday night ritual every red blooded American male dreams of fulfilling. Waits I'm assuming had this one down pat. Talk about dead ends in life, Fumblin' With The Blues is like loserville 101, it's all so easy to start it's just the finishing that's the damn hard part to create every weekend 52 times a year. Please Call Me, Baby is a bit more sentimental that the other songs here as Waits does his best Frank Sinatra thing, he sings, \\"If I exercise my devils well my angles my leave too, when they leave they're so hard to find.\\" Depot, Depot brings us into the bus station as Waits watches the people come and go. Did this guy ever go home in 1974 or was he just a street bum himself? By the time Drunk on The Moon hits you, you have surrendered, a few beers in and feeling lonely as hell even in a crowd. The album closes with, The Ghosts of Saturday Night (After Hours At Napoleone's Pizza House) which takes the listener on a late night excursion of observational destitution that began with dreams but ends the same every time with, \\"Leaving the town in the keeping of the one who is sweeping up the Ghosts of Saturday Night.\\" The album is best served during late nights, reflective thinking, and tends to sound best when you're on the bottom of life instead of the top. Music doesn't get any cooler than this, will never be hipper than this, and for all intense and purposes will never be as free to roam the late night American byways of days gone by, ever again. Go to the thrift store and buy yourself an old suit jacket that smells like liquor and cheap women, and when you listen to this put it on and you'll be ready to experience the music the way it was lived.

Gabriel's story of Rael, a New York City street kid coming of age was perfect for the bands temperament, their stage show, and most importantly, public consumption. No progressive album should ever be limited to a single album and that is why this remains the ultimate progressive masterpiece spread out over four sides of elegantly encrypted vinyl. Perhaps this is the best progressive statement of all time, there may be doubters but they haven't listen to all four sides at once, if they did they would come away from the experience shaken, breathless, and embarrassed for not being part of the all-knowing ones. The songs are like mini movies that strike a chord between escaping adolescence and floating through the experience with discoveries greater than those of the earliest world travelers. The package itself is up to the expectations of the music with its six cover pictures of Rael in different song set images and as you open the gate fold cover the story of Rael is scrolled across the inside like some biblical constitution of youthful affirmation and enlightenment. The playing is heart stopping, soulful, distant, flawless and above all deliciously perfect at every note. The songs slither at one moment and then grandly parade by in the next. Cuckoo Cocoon, Hairless Heart, Counting Out Time, The Carpet Crawlers, The Chamber of 32 Doors, Lilywhite Lilith and Anyway all show pop rock sensibility that thumb their noses at the standard contrite limitations. The Waiting Room just might be the scariest piece of music ever recorded although the group says the first unrecorded version was infinitely more creepy. Progressively indulgent numbers like, In The Cage, Fly On The Windshield, The Lamia, The Colony Of the Slippermen and Here Comes The Supernatural Anesthetist are all filled with mellotron, guitar and bass runs that show years of hard fraught nights figuring out the difference between really good and really not good enough, this time they got it all right and perhaps better than right, perfect. This is all so serene and yet so grand all at the same time, every listen for me over 30 plus years has produced a new discovery, another cord your amazed by, a lyric that suddenly makes since and a coupling of instrumental passages that seem to line up spiritually for the very first time. When Genesis found this rare apocalypse of time, talent, resources, energy and indulgence they did not stop until they made the best of it. It takes you all the way there, through their vision, out the other side, with little if anything left to listen to of equal value in your future. The birth of this album was the demise of the band, but lucky for you there is always tomorrow and another chance to listen to The Lamb Lies Down On Broadway. So go to sleep and dream of Slippermen, Carpet Crawlers, Hairless Hearts, Lamia's and all things that will never be again, an album so amazingly immaculate as this that time, space and perception become the bright light of inclusion and you my friend are hopelessly wrapped in the cuckoo.

It's a good set and you can hear the hunger of the band and the virtuoso playing already. And this disc sounds like an upcoming band.  And thinking back, yeah....these guys were and still are, the REAL DEAL.

B00007L4MJ|4.0|Very Art Deco, very strange, but historically vital and also pretty interesting.<br /><br />Metropolis takes place in 2026, but you have to remember that it is as imagined by 1927. I believe it was Robert Heinlein (or maybe Isaac Asimov?) who said every science fiction story has three times: the year it was written, the year in which it is set, and the year in which the reader/viewer experiences it. Especially when we are far closer to the year imagined than the year in which the imagining was done, the more slack we have to cut the writer/director.<br /><br />Metropolis is divided into three areas. On the top, both physically and metaphorically, are the pleasure gardens and stately homes of the privileged class. The idle rich, for the most part, but also people like the Master of Metropolis and his immediate staff. In the middle, only a little underground, are the machines. This is where the workers work, but where they live is much further underground.<br /><br />One day, the son of John Fredersen, the Master of Metropolis, was in a pleasure garden chasing a teasing girl when a lovely young woman appeared at the door surrounded by workers' children, dressed in rags and looking pitiful. 1846612950|5.0|FROM EXPERIENCED PRACTITIONERS TO LITIGANTS IN PERSON: A STEP-BY-STEP GUIDE TO JUDICIAL REVIEW<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />As Stephen Sedley reminds us in the Foreword to the second edition of this book, judicial review of governmental acts and omissions didn't even feature in the Bar examinations in the 1960s!<br /><br />Yet now, judicial review is `centre-stage in both legal theory and legal practice' -- and this is not the only distinctive aspect of judicial review. Another is the central and salient fact that in judicial review, it's the state that is usually one of the parties. Thus the purpose of judicial review, at least in theory, is to hold open the option for individuals to challenge the might of the state.<br /><br />From the signing of Magna Carta in 1215 -- as the authors point out in the preface -- the common law has developed (not without struggle) to protect fundamental rights and freedoms.  So it is curious that it's only within the last half century or so that judicial review has been formally instituted to uphold these rights.<br /><br />Judicial review, then, is intended to ensure that decisions made by the state that affect the lives of individuals -- or groups --are both democratic and lawful. The stated aim of the expert authors of this Guide, recently published by Jordans, has therefore been to assist access to judicial review on the part of individuals and their advisers.<br /><br />`We hope', they say, `that both experienced lawyers and litigants in person will be able to rely on this book to guide them through the process.'<br /><br />The book is divided into four parts.  The first - Law and Practice - guides the reader step by step though the complexities of the judicial review process, from initial instructions to appeals and ultimately, costs. As in many another legal process, costs are the caveat emptor and anyone contemplating a resort to judicial review should bear in mind the expense that is likely to ensue.<br /><br />Parts II, III and IV offer respectively, a procedural checklist, forms and precedents (twenty in all) and finally, a guide to the relevant legislation and protocol.<br /><br />The second edition cites a number of new developments in law and practice; developments in costs and funding being one of them. Other new material focuses on such issues as changes to appeals (CPR Part 52)... ongoing developments in case law... and new limitations in the context of immigration.<br /><br />These of course are only a few examples. As one would expect, this reliable, concise and readable work of reference contains extensive tables of cases, statutes, statutory instruments and European and international material, plus a 17-page index at the back.<br /><br />From students and academics, to experienced and inexperienced practitioners alike, anyone interested in this complex but fascinating subject -- or professionally involved in it -- should rush out and acquire this book as a matter of priority. 1423140591|3.0|Okay, let me start by saying that I have been a fan of Rick Riordan's books for about six years now, since around the time Sea of Monsters came out. I've read the old Percy Jackson series multiple times and am also following the Kane Chronicles. So naturally, a little over a year ago when Lost Hero came out I was ecstatic, and after borrowing it from my younger brother I was left with a feeling of disappointment. Not one to give up hope though, when Son of Neptune came out in October I had already pre-ordered it and upon arrival read the entire thing in about a day. At first, I was thrilled. The plot was exciting, the Roman Camp Jupiter and the minor characters were all incredibly interesting and full of personality, the new female protagonist, Hazel, was vastly more sympathetic and likable than Piper, and, best of all, Percy Jackson was back! But as a the next few days passed and I had time to mull over and digest what I had just read, the greater the problems I initially had with the book seemed to get, and additional flaws seemed to surface. So now, months later and immediately following a re-read, I'm reviewing The Son of Neptune.<br /><br />To start, the plot:<br /><br />Son of Neptune follows RR's standard formula: some event outside of camp takes place, demigod(s) go to camp and some quick event happens there, demigods get a quest and leave camp, demigods run into complications getting to their destination (monsters, detours, ect...), demigods get to destination and have a fight with the Big Bad of the week (book), demigods return to camp. Throughout the journey, readers are see events, objects, and people from Greek, and now Roman, mythology. Now I want to put out that this formula is not a bad thing, with the extensive mythology RR works with the plot always manages to stay vaguely interesting. There are two problems though. First, after so many novels of this, we know the characters are going to successfully complete their mission and all come out of it alive, so we never feel any suspense or strong excitement when they are \\"in danger\\", just a simple curiosity (if anything) in how they are going to get out of it. Second, at least a quarter of the events Percy, Hazel, and Frank go through on their quest seem completely inconsequential to the plot of SoN, let alone the overarching series' plot. Some fans thought that these chapters were funny, I personally found them rather dull, and a waste of paper.<br /><br />I will admit that the events that happened at Camp Jupiter were all highly entertaining, and were mostly relevant to the book and series as a whole and those that weren't were at least decent examples of world-building. Riordan does seem to be attempting to make romance a more essential plot point this time, which can be either something you love or hate, but unfortunately (for me at least), there is actually more going on there than in the gods v. giants-good v. evil department. It also seems to be a mix and match version of other RR novels' plots; the quest is a rescue mission (like in Sea of Monsters, Titan's Curse, and The Lost Hero), the characters face off against \\"evil\\" people working for the main Big Bad (like in TLH), and fight two giants (again like in TLH) all the while racing against the clock. And there are continuity errors: you know how in TLH it was December and Percy had been missing three days? In Son of Neptune it's mentioned multiple times that he (and Jason) went missing around October, or \\"eight months ago\\". In a series were time and deadlines matter so much, this is a major problem. My overall grade: 2/5<br /><br />Next, the main Characters (I'll attempt to avoid spoilers but I've always been really bad a gauging what constitutes one):<br /><br />1) Percy: well, he's back. In third person, which can be a hurdle for PJatO fans to get over, but he is basically the same guy. Still incredibly powerful, loyal, goofy, and with the same sense of humor (though it is muffled by the third person and amnesia). His interaction with Octavian is hilarious, and he and Hazel definitely seem to be developing a brother-sister relationship. The connection to Reyna is fascinating, and all the references to the PJatO books are a nice bonus for long-time fans. The way he immediately beings to think of the Roman camp as home and family is totally in line with his character, but while I found it touching, others (especially those who prefer Camp Half-Blood) may find it startling and annoying. Really my only complaint is the over-stressing and portrayal of his relationship with Annabeth; who he remembers as, not one of his best and oldest friends, but as his girlfriend. They were only dating a few months before he was kidnapped by Hera/Juno, and yet he's actively considering starting a family with her in this novel and thinking \\"romantic\\" (gushy) thoughts about her- only one of which was about her personality, ambitions, and/or interests- and almost all of which were Out of Character for people who have read the first series. Percabeth shippers will find his thoughts and comments delightful, of course, but they'll be an annoyance for anyone who dislikes the pairing or Annabeth, or is ambivalent about Annabeth or Percabeth. Finally, he doesn't really get any character development (for those who have read the first series- those who are beginning with Heroes of Olympus might find him one of the best developed characters), but based on what the gods, Gaia, and giants are saying, he's going to be going through a rough time in the next three books that'll give him plenty of opportunity to grow. Overall grade: 4/5<br /><br />2)Hazel: the female character. Okay, so apparently everyone in the new series most be related biologically to someone else, and Hazel is no exception. Luckily, she breaks from the stereotype of what people imagine kids of this god to be like without becoming an antithesis of that god's values and role, and the relationship with her sibling seems genuinely loving (though it does undo some of the character development said sibling got in the previous series). Her powers are, like other members of the seven, insanely strong, but seem to have a downside (she accurately dubs it a curse) which helps make her more relatable, though we never see this curse harming her, the quest, or her allies except in flashbacks. Her flashbacks, which could have been a huge liability if they happened in the middle of a battle, or could have been used to create brilliant tension and genuine conflict, are only used as info-dump sessions and RR conveniently makes them disappear completely after he's finished telling us her back story. But she is far less boy-crazed than Piper, and seems to acknowledge and seek to rectify mistakes she makes. She doesn't seem to have any (in-universe-acknowledged) flaws though, which are what make characters people. Overall grade: 3/5<br /><br />3)Frank: the final third. Let me put this out there: by the end of my first read-through, I disliked Frank. That opinion hasn't changed in the seven months. I thought he was overpowered even by the seven's standards, and the only way he can be redeemed in my eyes is if that plot-device stick Riordan uses in place of developing actual flaws with Frank ends up burning and Frank dies (spoiler: this doesn't happen in SoN). The guy started out fine; un-claimed, clumsy, awkward, legion outcast with low-self esteem and not really good at anything except archery. Then, as the novel progresses, we see those flaws don't affect anything or are just Frank complaining (which he does a lot). Furthermore he learns to use his special ability in the space of days when the rest of the characters needed months, even years, to reach an equivalent skill level. For someone who likes consistency in their series, this is incredibly frustrating. What are things I actually enjoyed about him? Well he does make some good jokes, and I did enjoy his chapters at first (until about half-way through the book actually). Overall grade: 2/5.<br /><br />Gaia: I find her personally more intimidating after this book, but considering her threat level seemed laughable in The Lost Hero, that's not saying much. Her plan has interested me though, and the way she interacts differently with each hero and the various ways she attempts to manipulate them were fascinating. Overall Grade: 2/5<br /><br />Side Characters:<br /><br />The Gods: As always, they stole whatever seen they appeared in and managed to be some of the best characters in the book. Some major gods from the last series even received a bit of character development. Overall grade: 5/5<br /><br />Reyna: By the end of the novel we still don't know much about her, but she still manages to be one of the best written characters in the novel. Her personality is fairly complex and her priorities seem to be straight, and every bit of her dialogue flows smoothly- which is saying something as at times dialogue felt very forced in this book. Overall grade: 5/5<br /><br />Octavian: This guy was brilliant. I would totally read an entire book just about him. His interactions with Percy were hilarious, and his dealings with the senate and Reyna had me surprised (that Riordan would write a morally grey character like this) and hopeful, as those scenes tended to be well written. Yet I fear that we were supposed to dislike this character, not be amused by him (he's obviously set up to be an antagonist). Still, it gives me hope. Overall Grade: 4/5 (because if an author attempts to set up a potential villain- definitely at least an antagonist- I should not like him more than the main characters).<br /><br />Everyone Else: 2/5. Really, there's not much to say, besides Dakota and one of Gaia's servants everyone else was barely developed. More like moving, talking background to be honest. None of them even get last names! This wouldn't be a problem, except we're supposed to dislike the giants (and see them as threats to our protagonists) and like Dakota and Gwen and the amazons. Except by the end, nothing. They're just there, and I can't care about characters aren't developed more than the bare shells of stereotypes. Really, the only reason this wasn't a 1/5 was Dakota, one of the giants, and the previously mentioned servant.<br /><br />And finally the last part: the writing itself! Overall, it wasn't bad. There were definitely parts where sentences should have been joined together and the writing could seem choppy at times. I could always tell who was narrating though. Riordan should also invest in a thesaurus because he repeated select words a lot, and sentences should have varied in length more. He also needs to use far less \\"he\\"s and \\"she\\"s. Overall Grade: 3/5.<br /><br />So there's my lengthy criticism of the next book from an author I've been reading for years. Were there problems? Obviously I think so. Does that mean I didn't enjoy it? Not in the slightest; I enjoyed nearly every minute and will probably re-read it in autumn before Mark of Athena (the next book in the series) comes out. Should you read it? That depends, I think. If you liked The Lost Hero, you would probably actually enjoy this a lot more than I did, so you should certainly read it. And if you have read and enjoyed Percy Jackson and the Olympians then it's probably worth a shot, but if you didn't like The Lost Hero (whether you have read the Percy Jackson series or not) I would recommend reading the sample chapter and basing your purchase off how much you enjoyed that. It pretty much sets the level for all of Percy's chapters, which make up a third of the book. Anyone who hasn't read The Lost Hero or the Percy Jackson books: go read one of them. I recommend The Lightning Thief, but in the end it's up to you. I hope this has been of some help.ing (though it does undo some of the character development said sibling got in the previous series). B007J4T2G8|4.0|I cannot stress this enough...these books are not written for teens or young adults who might be easily influenced.<br /><br />Well, after skimming through other reviews of Fifty Shades of Grey on Goodreads, I admit to being a little nervous in writing this review. Quite honestly, outside of a full-on book report, book reviewing is a subjective matter, meaning that what appeals to me, might not appeal to you and vice versa...that being said, I, personally, enjoyed EL James' writing, often finding myself laughing out loud because of humor or the sheer absurdity of any given situation.<br /><br />When you take time to stop and think, how many of the books you read are, or are destined to be, classic literary works of art? I understand that most of what I read is a type of pop-culture and serves, at best, as a lovely escape and form of immense entertainment. The same thing can be said about movies and television but rarely do these two cause as much heated debate and mud-slinging as books do. I wonder why that is?<br /><br />So, what can I tell you about Fifty Shades of Grey that you don't already know. Very little, I'd guess. Yes, it was originally written as fan-fic based around the Twilight Series. A very adult piece of fan-fic, I'll give you, but fan-fic all the same. Christian, a control-freak, ego-maniac, falls for Anastasia, a plain looking, clumsy college student. Remind you of another love/hate couple?<br /><br />Anastasia, quite literally, stumbles into Christian Grey's office on a mission for her school newspaper. Her roommate, the editor, is ill and Ana is forced to interview the illusive and powerful businessman in Kate's place. The first words out of Ana's mouth lead Mr. Grey to believe that Ana is a natural submissive. He stalks her, traces her cell phone, and becomes easily angered when other men come near Ana and this is all before his proposed relationship...if that's even what you could call such an arrangement. He couldn't be more wrong, and much to his surprise, he likes Ana's feistiness and smart mouth. He likes that she challenges him.<br /><br />Unlike Bella, Anastasia doesn't go leaping into anything head first. She listens to Christian's proposal and does her research before deciding to give his \\"lifestyle\\" a chance. It doesn't take either of them long to figure out she's not cut out to be a submissive and could it be that Christian actually wants something other than what he's always had? Ana's realization and walk-of-shame out of his life may be the thing Christian needs to control his inner control-freak. What a conundrum!<br /><br />Yes, this book is taking over the media. Yes, I gave into the hype and read it. Yes, I saw the similarities between this novel and Twilight. Yes, I am a Twilight fanatic and I'm not ashamed. Yes, I also enjoyed reading E.L. James' writing and Christian and Ana's relationship. The book picked up pace significantly when the highly acclaimed e-mails began flying back and forth between the couple. I kind of loved that Ana could be so honest with Christian from behind a computer screen. Reminded me a little of myself and, before we judge, aren't we all a little braver when cloaked?<br /><br />Would I recommend Fifty Shades of Grey to my friends? Only with a strong cautionary warning about the amount of, not only sexual content, but also of the BDSM content. Also, Christian is fervently screwed in the head...I haven't figured it all out yet, but I suspect his childhood sucked the normalness out of him, because while he's drop dead, smoldering sexy, he is also demented.<br /><br />While I always love comments, and I do, please follow the Golden Rule and do as you would have done to you.
